According to al-Uyun, what is the condition for witnesses who heard a confession but did not see the confessor?

8 min
Condition of Seeing the Confessor for Testimony on Confession
Lays down condition of seeing the confessor for testimony on iqrar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of seeing the confessor (ru’yat al-muqirr) for testimony on iqrar and apply it to scenarios involving identification after death or reliance on two just men.

Core Idea
Testimony on confession requires having seen the person – hearing alone is insufficient to prevent fraud.

In Hanafi fiqh, witnessing a confession (shahadah ‘ala al-iqrar) demands more than just hearing the words. The witness must have visual knowledge of the confessor. This lesson explores that condition and its exceptions, including reliance on two just men for identification and the case of a deceased confessor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage opens with 'ويشترط في الشهادة بالإقرار رؤية المقر' – seeing the confessor is a condition for testimony on confession. This is the core principle.
2
Analyze the examples
Work through al-Khassaf's example (man in a house with no other exit, previously seen) and the case in al-‘Uyun (hidden witnesses). Note when testimony is permitted (previous seeing) and when it is not (hearing only, without seeing).
The key distinction is whether the witness had visual contact at some point.
3
Apply the condition to women
Al-Jami‘ al-Saghir requires seeing the woman's face. The text mentions an incident where the Imam ordered her to uncover her face. This shows the strictness of the condition.
4
Examine identification after death
If the confessor dies, witnesses need two just men to testify to her lineage, since direct pointing is no longer possible. For a living confessor, pointing suffices.
This distinguishes between identification by pointing and by testimony.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa requires widespread knowledge (tawatur) for identification via others, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) accept two just men. The fatwa is on the companions' view ('وعليه الفتوى').
This ikhtilaf affects how a witness can identify an unknown confessor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشترط في الشهادة بالإقرار رؤية المقر لما في شهادات البزازية. وذكر الخصاف : رجل في بيت وحده ودخل عليه رجل ورآه ثم خرج وجلس على الباب وليس للبيت مسلك غيره فسمع إقراره من الباب بلا رؤية وجهه حل له أن يشهد بما أقر . وفي العيون رجل خبأ قوماً لرجل ثم سأله عن شيء فأقروهم يسمعون كلامه ويرونه وهو لا يراهم جازت شهادتهم وإن لم يروه وسمعوا كلامه لا تحل لهم الشهادة ا هـ . وفي الجامع الصغير شرط رؤية وجه المرأة ورأيت الإمام خالي أمرها بكشف الوجه وأمرها بالخروج ويؤيده ما في العيون كذا في الخلاصة. وفي جامع الفصولين: حسرت عن وجهها وقالت أنا فلانة بنت فلان بن فلان وهبت لزوجي مهري فلا يحتاج الشهود إلى شهادة عدلين أنها فلانة بنت فلان ما دامت حية إذ يمكن الشاهد أن يشير إليها، فإن ماتت فيحنئذ يحتاج الشهود إلى شهادة عدلين بنسبها . وقال قبله : لو أخبر الشاهد عدلان أن هذه المقرة فلانة بنت فلان يكفي هذا للشهادة على الاسم والنسب عندهما وعليه الفتوى
It is a condition for testimony on confession that the witness see the confessor, as stated in the Shahadat of al-Bazzaziyyah. Al-Khassaf mentioned: A man was alone in a house, another man entered and saw him, then left and sat at the door—the house had no other exit. He heard his confession from the door without seeing his face; it was permissible for him to testify to what he confessed. In al-‘Uyun: A man hid some people for another man, then asked him about something, and they came to know his statement while hearing his speech and seeing him, though he did not see them. Their testimony was permitted. If they did not see him but heard his speech, testifying is not permissible for them. In al-Jami‘ al-Saghir: It is a condition to see the face of a woman. I saw my maternal uncle the Imam ordering her to uncover her face and to go out; this is supported by what is in al-‘Uyun, as in al-Khulasah. In Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: She uncovered her face and said, 'I am so-and-so daughter of so-and-so, I have gifted my dower to my husband,' so the witnesses do not need two just men to testify that she is so-and-so as long as she is alive, since the witness can point to her. If she dies, then the witnesses need two just men to testify to her lineage. He said previously: If two just men inform the witness that this confessor is so-and-so daughter of so-and-so, this suffices for testimony on the name and lineage according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a definition and elaboration of the conditions for testimony on confession (shahadah ‘ala al-iqrar) in Hanafi fiqh. The general rule is that the witness must have seen the person making the confession; hearing alone is insufficient. This is supported by several examples: al-Khassaf’s case of a man hearing a confession from behind a door without seeing the face—the testimony is valid because he had previously seen the person. However, if the witness only hears the voice without ever seeing the person, the testimony is not accepted. For a woman, the condition includes seeing her face, as clarified in al-Jami‘ al-Saghir. The text then discusses identification: when the confessor is alive and present, the witness can point to her, so no additional proof of identity is needed. After her death, two just men must testify to her lineage. The passage also records that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), if two just men inform the witness of the confessor’s name and lineage, this is sufficient for the witness to testify, and this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The reasoning is that if a judge can base a judgment on the report of two just men, then a witness can also rely on their report for identification. This lesson emphasizes that testimony on confession requires both auditory and visual knowledge of the confessor, with exceptions for practical situations like hidden witnesses or pointing to a living person.

Key Terms
رؤية المقر (ru’yat al-muqirr): The witness must have seen the person making the confession at some point before testifying, either directly or through previous acquaintance.
الشهادة بالإقرار (ash-shahadah bil-iqrar): Testimony on confession: bearing witness to a person's admission of a right or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is required for a witness to testify on a confession (iqrar)?
A) Only hearing the confession clearly
B) Seeing the face of the confessor
C) Having written documentation of the confession
D) The presence of a judge

Key Takeaway
Testimony on confession requires both auditory and visual identification of the confessor, with the relied-upon position accepting two just men for identification when direct seeing is impossible.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
6 min
Ruling When Confessor Says 'Do Not Testify'
Rules whether a witness may testify after being told not to by the confessor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testifying after a confessor’s prohibition and distinguish it from the case where the claimant forbids.

Core Idea
A witness may testify even if the confessor forbids, but not if the claimant forbids.

In Hanafi law, testimony is a communal duty (fard kifayah), not a personal favor. This lesson examines a specific scenario: the confessor (muqirr) tells the witness not to testify. Does this prohibition bind the witness? The answer depends on who is speaking—the confessor or the claimant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Distinguish between the confessor (muqirr) who admitted a right, and the claimant (mudda'i) who asserts that right. Their roles affect the ruling.
2
State the general rule
From Abu Hanifa: the witness may testify despite the confessor's prohibition. The text says 'ينبغي لهم أن يشهدوا' (they should testify).
3
Note the exception
If the claimant says 'do not testify', the witness should not testify. The claimant may be dropping his claim.
4
Examine the dissenting view
Hasan al-Basri and Hasan ibn Ziyad held that the witness should not testify when the confessor forbids. The Hanafi school rejects this.
5
Consider the hila
The passage also presents a legal stratagem: if the confessor fears his confession will be used against him, he may make a statement through an intermediary without personal liability.
This is a separate ruling, but relevant to the context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن المقر إذا قال للشاهد لا تشهد علي بما سمعته فله أن يشهد عليه إلا إذا قال له المدعي لا تشهد عليه؛ ذكره في حيل التاتارخانية من حيل المداينات معزياً إلى الخصاف حملاً على أنه مبطل في دعواه لكن نقل بعده الاختلاف فيما لو جاء المدعي بعد النهي وطلب من الشاهد الشهادة فليراجع . وأما الحسن البصري والحسن بن زياد فإنهما يقولان لا يشهدون به قال الفقيه. وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال : ينبغي لهم أن يشهدوا وبه نأخذ ا هـ. ثم قال بعده قال الفقيه : إن كان يخاف على نفسه أنه إذا أقر بشيء صدق وادعى أن شريكه قبض لا يصدقه يقول للمتوسط اجعل كان هذا المال على غيري وأنا أعبر عنه ثم يقول قبض كذا وكذا فيبين الجميع من غير أن يضيف إلى نفسه كيلا يصير حجة عليه ا هـ.
Then know that if the confessor says to the witness 'Do not testify against me based on what you have heard,' he may testify against him, unless the claimant says 'Do not testify against him.' This is mentioned in Hiyal al-Tatar Khaniyyah from the strategies of debts, attributed to al-Khassaf, on the basis that he (the confessor) is false in his claim. However, it is reported after it that there is disagreement if the claimant came after the prohibition and asked the witness to testify—so refer to it. As for Hasan al-Basri and Hasan ibn Ziyad, they say they should not testify. Al-Faqih said: It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he said: They should testify, and we adopt this. Then after it, al-Faqih said: If he fears for himself that when he confesses something, he will be believed and claim that his partner received it, the partner will not be believed. He should tell an intermediary: 'Make this money as belonging to someone else, and I will express it.' Then he says 'I received such and such,' and he explains everything without attributing it to himself so that it does not become a proof against him.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on whether a witness may testify after the confessor (muqirr) explicitly asks him not to. The general rule according to Abu Hanifa and relied upon by the Hanafi school is that the witness may still testify, because the right of testimony belongs to Allah and the community, not to the individual. The exception is if the claimant (plaintiff) tells the witness not to testify, then the witness should not, as the plaintiff may be dropping his claim. This is based on the principle that the confessor might be trying to avoid liability fraudulently. The text also records a dissenting view of Hasan al-Basri and Hasan ibn Ziyad, who prohibit testifying in such cases, but the Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa. Additionally, a practical hila (legal stratagem) is provided: if the confessor fears that his confession will be used against him in a way that harms him (e.g., if he is sued for something else), he can avoid direct confession by making a statement through an intermediary, attributing the matter to a third party without admitting personal liability.

Key Terms
لا تشهد علي (la tashhad 'alayya): A phrase used by the confessor to forbid the witness from testifying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man confesses to a debt and then tells the witness 'Do not testify against me,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The witness must not testify
B) The witness may still testify
C) The witness must ask the judge's permission
D) The witness is obligated to testify

Key Takeaway
A witness may testify despite a confessor's prohibition because testimony is a communal duty, but must refrain if the claimant forbids.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony on Name and Lineage Using Two Just Men
Intra-Hanafi dispute on identification for testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on establishing a party's name and lineage for testimony and apply the mufta bihi view in a practical scenario.

Core Idea
The two companions allow identification by two just men; Abu Hanifa requires tawatur. The fatwa follows the companions.

When a witness testifies in court, they must correctly identify the parties. But how do they know the name and lineage? This lesson examines a dispute within the Hanafi school: can a witness rely on the report of two just men, or must the identification be based on widespread knowledge? The answer affects the validity of countless testimonies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Understand what is being disputed: the method for a witness to ascertain the name and lineage of a party before testifying.
The witness does not need to personally know the party; they may rely on others.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa requires tawatur (widespread reputation) to prevent fraud. A mere report of two just men is insufficient.
Tawatur means knowledge that is generally accepted and cannot be collusively fabricated.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani accept the report of two just men, drawing an analogy (qiyas) with judicial testimony itself.
They argue: if a judge can base a verdict on the testimony of two just men, then a witness can rely on them for identification a fortiori.
4
Examine the qiyas argument
The analogy is based on the principle that judgment is higher in authority than testimony; what is allowed for the judge is allowed for the witness by primary inference.
The text states: 'والقضاء فوق الشهادة فتجوز الشهادة بإخبارهما بالطريق الأولى'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the two companions, as stated in the text: 'وعليه الفتوى'.
This means in practice, a witness may testify after being informed by two just men.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عرفهما باسمهما ونسبهما عدلان ينبغي للعدلين أن يشهدا الفرع على شهادتهما فيشهد عند القاضي عليها بالاسم والنسب وبالحق أصالة ا هـ . وأما حكم الحاكم فيصح أن يكون من قبيل المسموع بأن كان بالقول ويصح أن يكون من المرئيات إن كان فعلاً على ما قدمناه. وأما الغصب والقتل فلا يكونان إلا من المرئيات، ومن قصر البيع والإقرار والحكم على المرئيات فقد قصر والتحقيق ما أسمعتك ولو قال المؤلف ولو قال له لا تشهد علي بدل قوله وإن لم يشهد عليه لكان أفود لما في الخلاصة : لو قال المقر لا تشهد علي بما سمعت تسعه الشهادة ا هـ . فيعلم حكم ما إذا سكت بالأولى وإذا سكت يشهد بما علم ولا يقول أشهدني لأنه كذب وفي النوازل سئل محمد بن مقاتل عن شريكين يتحاسبان وعندهما قوم وقالا لا تشهدوا علينا بما تسمعونه منا ثم أقر أحدهما لصاحبه بشراء أو باع شيئاً فطلب المقر له بعد ذلك منهم الشهادة قال : ينبغي لهم أن يشهدوا بذلك وهو قول محمد بن سيرين. وأما الحسن البصري والحسن بن زياد فإنهما يقولان لا يشهدون به قال الفقيه. وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال : ينبغي لهم أن يشهدوا وبه نأخذ ا هـ. ثم قال بعده قال الفقيه : إن كان يخاف على نفسه أنه إذا أقر بشيء صدق وادعى أن شريكه قبض لا يصدقه يقول للمتوسط اجعل كان هذا المال على غيري وأنا أعبر عنه ثم يقول قبض كذا وكذا فيبين الجميع من غير أن يضيف إلى نفسه كيلا يصير حجة عليه ا هـ.
If two just men know them (the parties) by name and lineage, the two just men should have the branch witness testify on their testimony, so he testifies before the judge on the name and lineage and on the right originally. The ruling of the judge may be of the audible type by speech, and may be of the visible type if it is an act, as we have presented. As for usurpation and killing, they are only of the visible type. Whoever restricts sale, confession, and judgment to visible things has been inadequate. The verification is what I have made you hear. If the author had said 'if he said to him do not testify against me' instead of 'even if he did not testify against him' it would be better. In al-Khulasah: If the confessor says 'do not testify against me regarding what you heard,' he may still testify. So the ruling when he remains silent is known by the primary inference. When he remains silent, he testifies to what he knows, and he does not say 'he had me testify' because that is a lie. In al-Nawazil, Muhammad ibn Muqatil was asked about two partners settling accounts with people present, and they said 'do not testify against us regarding what you hear from us.' Then one of them confessed to a purchase or sale, and later the person in whose favor the confession was made asked them to testify. He said: They should testify based on that, and this is the opinion of Muhammad ibn Sirin. As for Hasan al-Basri and Hasan ibn Ziyad, they say they should not testify. Al-Faqih said: It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he said: They should testify, and we adopt this. Then after it, al-Faqih said: If he fears for himself that when he confesses something he will be believed and then claim his partner collected, he will not be believed. He should tell an intermediary: 'Make this money as if it belongs to someone else, and I will express it.' Then he says 'I collected such and such,' and explains everything without attributing to himself so that it does not become a proof against him.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the identification of a party for testimony. The issue is: can a witness testify to the name and lineage of a person based solely on the report of two just men? According to Abu Hanifa, such identification requires widespread knowledge (tawatur) to avoid fraud. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the report of two just men is sufficient, by analogy (qiyas) with the acceptance of their testimony in court. The text states that this is the fatwa. The passage also reiterates the ruling on testimony when the confessor says 'do not testify' and includes a stratagem to avoid self-incrimination.

Key Terms
الاسم والنسب (al-ism wa al-nasab): Name and lineage of a person, which must be identified for testimony.
التواتر (al-tawatur): Widespread, continuous transmission that precludes collusion.
الفرع (al-far'): The branch witness, i.e., the one who testifies based on the report of others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how can a witness establish the name and lineage of a party for testimony?
A) Only if he has personal knowledge of the party since childhood
B) By the testimony of two just men
C) By asking the party directly
D) By examining official documents

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school allows a witness to identify a party based on the report of two just men, by analogy with judicial testimony.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Testimony When Told Not to Testify
Ruling on testimony when the acknowledger forbids witnessing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony when the acknowledger forbids, identify the distinction when the claimant forbids, and explain the reasoning.

Core Idea
A witness may testify despite the muqir's prohibition, but not if the claimant forbids.

When someone confesses to a debt or right in your presence and then tells you not to testify, can you still testify? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on testimony after a direct prohibition. The key is distinguishing who issued the prohibition: the acknowledging person (muqir) or the claimant (mudda'i). You will learn the relied-upon position of Abu Hanifa and how it differs from other early scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the three roles: the muqir (acknowledger), the mudda'i (claimant), and the shahid (witness). The prohibition can come from either the muqir or the mudda'i.
2
Distinguish the source of prohibition
The passage treats the two cases differently. If the muqir forbids, the witness may still testify. If the mudda'i forbids, there is disagreement among scholars.
The muqir is confessing against himself, so his prohibition does not block justice.
3
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ibn Sirin held that witnesses should testify despite the muqir's prohibition. This is the mufta bihi.
The text says 'wa bihi na'khudh' (we adopt this).
4
Apply the reasoning
The judge rules based on testimony, so testimony itself cannot be prevented by the one who confessed. The right to prevent belongs to the one who would be harmed (the claimant), not the acknowledger.
If the claimant forbids, there is a difference of opinion; the matter should be referred to a jurist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا يرى أنهما لو شهدا عند القاضي يقضي بشهادتهما والقضاء فوق الشهادة فتجوز الشهادة بإخبارهما بالطريق الأولى، فإن عرفهما باسمهما ونسبهما عدلان ينبغي للعدلين أن يشهدا الفرع على شهادتهما فيشهد عند القاضي عليها بالاسم والنسب وبالحق أصالة ا هـ . وأما حكم الحاكم فيصح أن يكون من قبيل المسموع بأن كان بالقول ويصح أن يكون من المرئيات إن كان فعلاً على ما قدمناه. وأما الغصب والقتل فلا يكونان إلا من المرئيات، ومن قصر البيع والإقرار والحكم على المرئيات فقد قصر والتحقيق ما أسمعتك ولو قال المؤلف ولو قال له لا تشهد علي بدل قوله وإن لم يشهد عليه لكان أفود لما في الخلاصة : لو قال المقر لا تشهد علي بما سمعت تسعه الشهادة ا هـ . فيعلم حكم ما إذا سكت بالأولى وإذا سكت يشهد بما علم ولا يقول أشهدني لأنه كذب وفي النوازل سئل محمد بن مقاتل عن شريكين يتحاسبان وعندهما قوم وقالا لا تشهدوا علينا بما تسمعونه منا ثم أقر أحدهما لصاحبه بشراء أو باع شيئاً فطلب المقر له بعد ذلك منهم الشهادة قال : ينبغي لهم أن يشهدوا بذلك وهو قول محمد بن سيرين. وأما الحسن البصري والحسن بن زياد فإنهما يقولان لا يشهدون به قال الفقيه. وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال : ينبغي لهم أن يشهدوا وبه نأخذ ا هـ. ثم قال بعده قال الفقيه : إن كان يخاف على نفسه أنه إذا أقر بشيء صدق وادعى أن شريكه قبض لا يصدقه يقول للمتوسط اجعل كان هذا المال على غيري وأنا أعبر عنه ثم يقول قبض كذا وكذا فيبين الجميع من غير أن يضيف إلى نفسه كيلا يصير حجة عليه ا هـ . . ثم اعلم أن المقر إذا قال للشاهد لا تشهد علي بما سمعته فله أن يشهد عليه إلا إذا قال له المدعي لا تشهد عليه؛ ذكره في حيل التاتارخانية من حيل المداينات معزياً إلى الخصاف حملاً على أنه مبطل في دعواه لكن نقل بعده الاختلاف فيما لو جاء المدعي بعد النهي وطلب من الشاهد الشهادة فليراجع .
Do you not see that if they both testify before the judge, he judges by their testimony, and judgment is above testimony, so testimony is valid by their informing by primary reasoning. If two just persons know them by name and lineage, the two just persons should bear witness to the branch upon their testimony, and he testifies before the judge upon it by name and lineage and the right originally. As for the judge's ruling, it can be of the type of audible if it is by speech, and can be of the visible if it is an action, as we have presented. As for usurpation and killing, they are only from the visible. Whoever restricts sale, acknowledgment, and ruling to the visible has restricted, and the verification is what I have made you hear. If the author said: 'If he said to him: do not testify against me' instead of his saying 'even if he did not make him witness', it would be more beneficial because in al-Khulasa: If the acknowledging person says: do not testify against me regarding what you heard, the witness may testify. So the ruling when he remains silent is known by primary reasoning. When he remains silent, he testifies to what he knows and does not say 'he made me witness' because it is a lie. In al-Nawazil, Muhammad ibn Muqatil was asked about two partners who were settling accounts with some people present, and they said: do not testify against us regarding what you hear from us. Then one of them acknowledged to his partner a purchase or sold something, and the person for whom the acknowledgment was made later asked them for testimony. He said: They should testify to that, and this is the saying of Muhammad ibn Sirin. As for al-Hasan al-Basri and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, they say they do not testify by it. Al-Faqih said: It is reported from Abu Hanifa that he said: They should testify, and we adopt this. Then he said afterwards: Al-Faqih said: If he fears for himself that if he acknowledges something he will be believed and claims that his partner received [payment], he is not believed. He says to the intermediary: make this wealth as if it belongs to someone else and I am expressing it, then he says: he received such and such, and he explains everything without attributing it to himself so that it does not become a proof against him. Then know that if the acknowledging person says to the witness: do not testify against me regarding what you heard, he may testify against him, unless the claimant says to him: do not testify against him. This is mentioned in Hiyal al-Tatar Khaniyya from the devices of debts, attributed to al-Khassaf, on the basis that he is false in his claim. However, after that, the difference is transmitted regarding if the claimant comes after the prohibition and asks the witness to testify, so let it be referred to.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on testimony when the person who made the acknowledgment (al-muqir) tells the witness not to testify. The main principle is that a witness can testify even if the person forbids him, unless the claimant (al-mudda'i) forbids him, because the claimant may be falsifying his claim. The passage begins with a general reasoning: if the judge can judge based on testimony, then testimony itself is permissible. The commentator explains that the judge's ruling can be based on audible or visible matters. Then he discusses the specific case from al-Khulasa: if the acknowledging person says 'do not testify against me', the witness may still testify. If the person remains silent, the witness testifies to what he knows but should not say 'he made me a witness' because that would be a lie. A story from al-Nawazil about two partners settling accounts illustrates the ruling: Muhammad ibn Muqatil and Muhammad ibn Sirin held that the witnesses should testify despite the prohibition, while al-Hasan al-Basri and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad disagreed. Abu Hanifa is reported to agree with the majority, and this is the relied-upon position. There is also a note about a trick (hila) to avoid liability in case the acknowledged person fears being sued. Finally, the key difference is clarified: if the muqir forbids, testimony is still valid; if the mudda'i forbids, there is a difference of opinion and the matter should be referred to a jurist.

Key Terms
المقر (al-muqir): The person who makes an acknowledgment or confession (iqrar) of a right against himself.
الشاهد (al-shahid): The witness who has seen or heard the event and is called to testify in court.
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The claimant, the person who asserts a right against another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if the claimant (al-mudda'i) tells the witness not to testify?
A) Witness cannot testify.
B) There is a difference of opinion; the case should be referred to a jurist.
C) Witness must testify.
D) Witness should seek permission from the acknowledger.

Key Takeaway
A witness may testify even if the acknowledging person forbids it, but if the claimant forbids, there is ikhtilaf and the matter requires juristic consultation.

Establishing Identity of Original Witnesses in Shahada ala Shahada: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Condition that branch witnesses know original witnesses' identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the number of witnesses needed to establish identity for shahada ala shahada, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah) of each position.

Core Idea
For shahada ala shahada, Abu Hanifa requires a large group; Sahibayn accept two just witnesses — the dispute turns on the standard of proof needed.

When a witness cannot attend court, Islamic law permits testimony based on the original witness's testimony (shahada ala shahada). But the branch witnesses must know the original witness's identity to prevent fraud. How many people must testify to that identity? This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, with the commentator offering a decisive analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What condition must branch witnesses satisfy regarding their knowledge of the original witnesses? The passage specifies they must know the name and lineage. But how is that knowledge established?
The dispute is about the number of people needed to testify to the original witness's identity.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa demands testimony from a large group whose collusion on falsehood is unimaginable (جماعة لا يتصور تواطؤهم على الكذب). This is a very high standard, meant to eliminate any chance of fraud.
This view is extremely cautious but may be impractical.
3
State Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the testimony of two just men (شهادة رجلين) is sufficient. They base this on qiyas (analogy) to all other rights (سائر الحقوق), where two witnesses are enough to prove a claim.
They argue that identity verification is like any other factual proof.
4
Analyse the commentator's tarjih
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym?) explicitly says: 'The judge obtains knowledge of lineage through two just witnesses, so the branch witnesses should also obtain it through two just witnesses.' This reasoning favours Sahibayn's view.
The commentator uses a qiyas argument from the judge's practice to the witnesses' situation.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa's view, it is very difficult to gather enough people to verify identity, potentially blocking many valid branch testimonies. Under Sahibayn's view, identity is easily established, facilitating testimony on testimony.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the view of Sahibayn due to its practicality and the commentator's support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فإن عرفهما باسمهما ونسبهما عدلان) في النسخ بضمير التثنية في الثلاثة والصواب حذفه والضمير للمؤنثة كما في جامع الفصولين وفيه : ولا يجوز الاعتماد على إخبار المتعاقدين باسمهما ونسبهما لعلهما تسميا وانتسبا باسم غيرهما ونسبه يريدان أن يزورا على الشهود ليخرجا المبيع من يد مالكه فلو اعتمد على قولهما نفذ تزويرهما وبطل أملاك الناس، وهذا فصل غفل عنه كثير من الناس فإنهم يسمعون لفظ الشراء والبيع والإقرار والتقابض من رجلين لا يعرفونهما، ثم إذا استشهدوا بعد موت صاحب البيع شهدوا على ذلك الاسم والنسب ولا علم لهم بذلك فيجب أن يحترز عن مثل ذلك وطريق علم الشهود بالنسب أن يشهد عندهم جماعة لا يتصور تواطؤهم على الكذب عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما شهادة رجلين كافٍ كما في سائر الحقوق أقول : يحصل للقاضي العلم بالنسب بشهادة عدلين فينبغي أن يحصل للشهود أيضاً بشهادة عدلين كما هو قولهما اهـ
His saying: (if two just persons know them by name and lineage). In the copies with the dual pronoun in the three, and the correct is to drop it and the pronoun is for the feminine as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. In it: It is not permissible to rely on the information of the two contracting parties about their name and lineage, because they may have named themselves and ascribed to a name and lineage of others, wanting to forge against the witnesses to take the sold item out of the hand of its owner. If one relied on their statement, their forgery would be executed and people's possessions would be nullified. This is a chapter that many people have been heedless of, for they hear the words of purchase, sale, acknowledgment, and receipt from two men they do not know. Then if they are asked to testify after the death of the seller, they testify to that name and lineage without any knowledge of it. So one must be cautious of such. The way for witnesses to know the lineage is that a group, whose collusion on falsehood is unimaginable, should testify before them according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the testimony of two men is sufficient, as in all other rights. I say: the judge obtains knowledge of lineage by the testimony of two just persons, so similarly the witnesses should obtain it by the testimony of two just persons, as is their statement.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn statement about the condition that the branch witnesses (who bear witness on the testimony of others) must know the original witnesses by name and lineage. The commentator explains that the correct reading in the matn is with the feminine pronoun referring to 'the two witnesses'. He then warns against relying on the parties themselves for identity verification because they might be lying to commit fraud. Many people are heedless of this: they hear transactions from strangers and later testify based on names and lineages without real knowledge. To establish identity, Abu Hanifa requires testimony from a large group whose collusion on a lie is impossible, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the testimony of two just men sufficient, just as in other claims. The commentator inclines towards the view of Sahibayn, arguing that since a judge obtains knowledge from two witnesses, the same should suffice for branch witnesses.

Key Terms
الشهادة على الشهادة (al-shahada ala al-shahada): Testimony of a branch witness based on the testimony of an original witness, used when the original witness cannot attend court.
العدالة (al-adalah): The quality of being just (adl), a condition for a witness's testimony to be accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which view on establishing the name and lineage of original witnesses is preferred?
A) Abu Hanifa's view requiring a large group
B) Al-Hasan al-Basri's view
C) The view of the two companions (Sahibayn)
D) There is no preferred view

Key Takeaway
The identity of original witnesses for shahada ala shahada must be independently verified; the preferred Hanafi view is that two just witnesses suffice, analogizing to other claims.

The Condition of Tahammul for Testimony on Testimony
Essential condition for testimony on testimony with rational proof and dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of tahammul for shahada ala shahada and explain the rationale and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding hearing in court.

Core Idea
Testimony on testimony requires formal charging (tahammul) because testimony only becomes proof in court.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the fundamental condition for a branch witness to testify on another's testimony. The author emphasizes that the branch witness must have heard the original witness actually testifying, not just any statement, and must have been formally charged (tahammul). The commentary also engages with a view from Fath al-Qadir that hearing in court might be sufficient, but the preferred Hanafi position insists on tahammul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is shahada ala shahada (testimony on testimony). The ruling is that it is conditionally valid; the primary condition discussed is tahammul.
2
Locate the primary condition
The text states: 'لا يشهد على شهادة غيره ما لم يشهد عليه' meaning the branch witness must have witnessed the original witness actually testifying. This requires direct auditory perception and formal charging (tahammul).
Tahammul is the formal charging of the branch witness by the original.
3
Understand the rationale
The reason given: testimony does not become a proof (hujja) except by transfer to the court. Therefore, the branch witness cannot make someone's speech a proof without authorization from the original witness.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Fath al-Qadir suggests that if the original testified in court, hearing that might suffice because the testimony is binding there. The commentator objects because binding only occurs upon the judge's ruling, which has not yet happened.
This shows the importance of the moment when testimony becomes a proof.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that tahammul is required; hearing in court without formal charging is not sufficient. The branch witness must be formally charged by the original.
This is based on the commentator's tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يشهد على شهادة غيره ما لم يشهد عليه لأنها لا تصير حجة إلا بالنقل إلى مجلس القاضي ولذا لا عدالة الأصول فلا يملك غيره أن يجعل كلامه حجة بلا أمره فلا بد من التحميل، وأفاد أنه لو سمعه يشهد أخر على شهادته لا أن يسعه يشهد لأنه إنما حمل غيره. وفي فتح القدير : وهذا الإطلاق يقتضي أنه لو سمعه يشهد في مجلس القاضي حل له أن يشهد على شهادته لأنها حينئذ ملزمة ا وفيه نظر لأنها لا تكون ملزمة إلا بالقضاء ولم يوجد. وترك المؤلف رحمه الله قيدين آخرين لجوازها على شهادة غيره : الأول أن
His saying: (And he should not testify on the testimony of another unless he has witnessed him testify, because it does not become a proof except by transfer to the court of the judge. Therefore, the justice of the originals is not [automatically] transferred, so no one else can make his speech a proof without his command. Thus, there must be formal charging (tahammul). And it indicates that if he heard him testifying, he testifies later on his testimony, not that he heard him testify because he only charged another. In Fath al-Qadir: This absolute statement implies that if he heard him testify in the court of the judge, it is permissible for him to testify on his testimony because it is then binding. But there is consideration because it is not binding except by judgment, which has not occurred. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) omitted two other conditions for the permissibility of testifying on the testimony of another: the first is that...
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage explains the core condition for shahada ala shahada (testimony on testimony): the branch witness must have heard the original witness actually testifying (يشهد عليه), not merely heard him saying something that could be testimony. The reason given is that testimony only becomes a binding proof when it is presented in court. Therefore, the branch witness cannot treat a statement as a proof without the original witness's authorization (tahammul). The commentator mentions a view from Fath al-Qadir that if the branch witness heard the original testify in court, that might suffice because the testimony is binding there, but he raises an objection: it only becomes binding upon the judge's ruling, which hasn't happened yet. The author (of Kanz al-Daqaiq) omitted two additional conditions for the permissibility of shahada ala shahada, the first of which is mentioned (but the text cuts off). This highlights the importance of the branch witness's direct auditory perception and the original witness's role in charging him.

Key Terms
التحميل (al-tahammul): The act of the original witness formally charging the branch witness to bear testimony on his behalf.
مجلس القاضي (majlis al-qadi): The court setting where testimony is presented and becomes a proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary condition for a branch witness to testify on the testimony of another?
A) He must see the original witness's written statement
B) He must hear the original witness testify and be formally charged (tahammul)
C) He must know the original witness well
D) He must have two witnesses confirm the original testimony

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires a branch witness to have heard the original witness testify and to have been formally charged (tahammul) for testimony on testimony to be valid.

Testimony on a Woman: Describing Her and Seeing Her Face
Ruling on describing a woman in court records and whether seeing her face is required for testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on describing a woman in court records and identify the condition of seeing her face for testimony, distinguishing between the mufta bihi and ihtiyat positions.

Core Idea
Seeing the woman's face is not required for testimony, but caution recommends it when bearing testimony on a veiled woman known only by voice.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two interrelated issues: the proper procedure for describing a woman in court records to minimize unnecessary looking, and the disputed requirement of seeing her face for bearing testimony. While the fatwa favors ease, there is a strong cautionary view that demands visual identification in certain cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural ruling
The passage begins with a note on court procedure: the judge should personally write the description (tahliya) of the woman to limit the number of men looking at her. This shows the importance of concealment (sitr).
The scribe should leave the description blank for the judge to fill.
2
State the core question
The passage then asks: 'Is seeing her face required?' This question refers to the validity of testimony about a woman's identity when the witness has not seen her face.
The answer is disputed within the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa is quoted saying testimony is not permissible until a group (jama'a) testifies that she is so-and-so. This implies that the witness does not need to see her face himself; identification by others suffices. The fatwa follows this view because it is easier for people.
The mufta bihi is that seeing the face is not a condition.
4
Analyze the exceptions
The passage then presents a scenario where the witness knows the woman by voice but never saw her face. The cautious view (ihtiyat) of some later scholars (Shams al-Islam, Zahir al-Din) requires seeing the face or person for bearing testimony (tahammul) in such cases.
This does not contradict the fatwa but adds a precaution.
5
Apply the distinction
If the witness had seen her face before she veiled, he can testify without re-seeing. If she was always veiled and he only knows her voice, the fatwa allows testimony with two informants, but ihtiyat requires seeing the face.
The passage ends with a reference from al-Nawadir that seeing the face is not required even then.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه من الفتاوى الصغرى من كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي : إذا كتب الكاتب محضر امرأة وأراد أن يحليها فإنه ينبغي له أن يترك موضع تحليتها حتى يكون القاضي هو الذي يحليها ويكتب تحليتها في المحضر أو يملي حليتها على الكاتب لأن الكاتب وإن حلاها لا يستغني القاضي عن النظر في وجهها فيكون فيه نظر رجلين إليها ولو حلاها القاضي كفي فيكون فيه نظر واحد وذلك أستر لها فكان أولى. وهل يشترط رؤية وجهها؟ ذكر الفقيه أبو الليث عن نصير بن يحيى قال : كنت عند أبي سليمان فدخل ابن محمد الحسن فسأله الشهادة عن على المرأة متى تجوز إذا لم يعرفها قال: كان أبو حنيفة يقول لا تجوز حتى يشهد عنده جماعة أنها فلانة وهو المختار للفتوى وعليه الاعتماد لأنه أيسر على الناس ا هـ . قوله: (وهل يشترط رؤية وجهها الخ) لم يذكر جواب الاستفهام وما ذكره بعده لا يصلح جواباً له، ولعل في العبارة سقطاً، وقد مر في هذه القولة عن الجامع الصغير اشتراطه وعبارة الخلاصة : وهل يشترط رؤية وجهها؟ اختلف المشايخ فيه منهم من لم يشترط وإليه مال الإمام خواهر زاده، وفي النوازل قال : يشترط رؤية شخصها. وفي الجامع الصغير : يشترط رؤية وجهها إلى آخر ما قدمه. وتقدم عن جامع الفصولين : لو أخبر الشاهد عدلان أنها فلانة بنت فلان يكفي للشهادة على الاسم والنسب عندهما وعليه الفتوى. قال أبو السعود فتحصل منه أن الفتوى على عدم اشتراط رؤية وجه المرأة اهـ . تنبيه : لا يخفى أن هذا كله عند عدم معرفته لها، أما إذا عرفها فيشهد عليها بدون رؤية وجهها، ولكن هذا ظاهر إذا رأى وجهها ثم تنقبت فشهد على إقرارها مثلاً في حال تنقبها فهذا لا شك أنه لا يحتاج إلى تعريف من غيره إذ تعريف غيره حينئذ لا يزيد على معرفته، وأما إذا كانت متنقبة وكان يعرفها قبل فعرفها بصوتها وهيئتها ولم ير وجهها وقت التنقب أو الإقرار فهل يكفي ذلك؟ ظاهر إطلاقهم أنه لا يكفي ففي العمادية قالوا: لا يصح التحمل بدون رأية وجهها وبه يفتي شمس الإسلام الأوزجندي وظهير الدين المرغيناني .اهـ. ولم يفصل بين ما إذا عرفها بصوتها أو لا . وفي البيري على الأشباه : لا يجوز أن يشهد على من سمعه من وراء حائط أو من فوق البيت وهو لا يراه وإن عرف كلامه لأن الكلام يشبه بعضه بعضاً كما في التتارخانية وفي منية المفتي: أقرت من وراء حجاب لا يجوز أن يشهد على إقرارها إلا إذا رأى شخصها ولم يشترط في النوادر رؤية وجهها
A note from al-Fatawa al-Sughra from the book 'al-Qadi ila al-Qadi': When a scribe writes a record of a woman and intends to describe her appearance, it is appropriate for him to leave the place of her description so that the judge is the one who describes her and writes her description in the record or dictates her description to the scribe, because even if the scribe describes her, the judge still needs to look at her face, and thus two men look at her; but if the judge describes her, one look suffices, and that is more concealing for her, so it is better. And is seeing her face required? Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth narrated from Nasir ibn Yahya: I was with Abu Sulayman when Ibn Muhammad al-Hasan entered and asked him about testimony regarding a woman: when is it permissible if he does not know her? He said: Abu Hanifa used to say it is not permissible until a group testifies before him that she is so-and-so, and that is the choice for fatwa and reliance is upon it because it is easier for the people. End. His saying: (And is seeing her face required, etc.) He did not mention the answer to the question, and what he mentioned after it is not suitable as an answer, and perhaps there is an omission in the expression. It has passed in this statement from al-Jami' al-Saghir the requirement, and the expression of al-Khulasah: And is seeing her face required? The mashayikh differed; among them is one who did not require it, and Imam Khaherzadeh inclined to that. And in al-Nawazil: He said: Seeing her person is required. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: Seeing her face is required, up to what he presented. And it preceded from Jami' al-Fusulayn: If two just witnesses inform the witness that she is so-and-so daughter of so-and-so, it suffices for testimony on the name and lineage according to them, and the fatwa is upon it. Abu al-Sa'ud said: Thus it results that the fatwa is on not requiring seeing the woman's face. End. Note: It is not hidden that all this applies when he does not know her. As for when he knows her, he testifies regarding her without seeing her face, but this is apparent when he saw her face then she veiled, and he testified about her acknowledgment, for example, while she was veiled; then there is no doubt that he does not need identification from others, since identification from others then does not add to his knowledge. However, if she was veiled and he knew her before, then recognized her by her voice and appearance but did not see her face at the time of veiling or acknowledgment, does that suffice? The apparent of their absolute statement is that it does not suffice. In al-'Imadiyyah they said: Bearing testimony is not valid without seeing her face, and Shams al-Islam al-Uzjandi and Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani give fatwa accordingly. End. And they did not differentiate between whether he recognized her by her voice or not. And in al-Biri 'ala al-Ashbah: It is not permissible to testify regarding someone he heard from behind a wall or from above the house while not seeing him, even if he recognizes his speech, because speech resembles each other, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. And in Munyat al-Mufti: A woman acknowledged from behind a veil; it is not permissible to testify about her acknowledgment unless he saw her person, and in al-Nawadir seeing her face was not required.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding testimony on a woman and the requirement of seeing her face. The passage begins with a note from al-Fatawa al-Sughra about proper procedure when a scribe writes a court record involving a woman: the scribe should leave the description of her appearance (tahliya) for the judge to write or dictate, because if the scribe describes her, two men (the scribe and the judge) would look at her, but if the judge does it, only one look is needed, which is more concealing and better. The passage then raises a key question: is seeing the woman's face required for the judge to testify about her identity? The author quotes a narration from Abu al-Layth that Abu Hanifa ruled it is not permissible until a group of witnesses testify that she is so-and-so (i.e., identification by others is needed), and this is the chosen position for fatwa because it is easier for people. The commentary notes that the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq left the question unanswered, and there is a possible omission. The mashayikh differed: some did not require seeing her face, while others required seeing her person (shakhs) or face. A compromise from Jami' al-Fusulayn says that if two just witnesses inform that she is so-and-so, it suffices for testimony on name and lineage, and that is the fatwa. Abu al-Sa'ud concludes that the fatwa is on not requiring seeing the woman's face. The passage then adds a crucial distinction: this ruling applies when the witness does not know the woman. If he knows her, he can testify without seeing her face—but that is if he had seen her face before she veiled. However, if she was always veiled and he recognizes her by voice and appearance but never saw her face, the majority say bearing testimony is not valid without seeing her face, according to al-'Imadiyyah and the fatwa of Shams al-Islam and Zahir al-Din. The passage ends with supporting references from al-Biri, al-Tatarkhaniyyah, and Munyat al-Mufti, noting that in al-Nawadir seeing her face was not required. This shows a nuanced Hanafi position: while the preferred fatwa is that seeing the face is not a condition, there is a strong ihtiyat (precaution) position for the witness to have actually seen the woman's face, especially when she is known by voice alone.

Key Terms
تحلية (tahliya): Describing a woman's physical appearance (her jewelry, features, etc.) in a court record for identification purposes.
رؤية الوجه (ru'ya al-wajh): Seeing the woman's face; a condition disputed among Hanafi scholars for the validity of testimony regarding her identity.
التحمل (al-tahammul): Bearing testimony (the act of witnessing an event or statement to later testify about it). In this context, bearing testimony on a woman's acknowledgment while she is veiled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred fatwa in this passage, what suffices for a witness to testify on a woman's identity without seeing her face?
A) His own recognition of her voice.
B) The testimony of two just witnesses that she is so-and-so.
C) A written description of her appearance.
D) His prior acquaintance with her family.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa does not require seeing the woman's face for testimony, but ihtiyat recommends it when the woman is veiled and only known by voice.

Shahadat — Testimony

Conditions for Testimony on Testimony: Acceptance and Prohibition
Explaining conditions and dispute on acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid testimony on testimony and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the branch's acceptance is a condition.

Core Idea
The branch must accept the imposition, or the testimony fails – but the Hanafi founders differ on why.

This lesson unpacks the first two conditions for testimony on testimony from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. You will see how Imam Muhammad differs from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whether the branch's rejection invalidates his ability to testify later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition
The text states that the branch witness must accept the imposition (tahmeel). If he says 'I do not accept', his subsequent testimony is not accepted.
This is based on the early part of the passage.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad views the act as tawkeel (agency), so rejection nullifies. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf view it as tahmeel (imposition), so rejection does not nullify.
The text says 'yambaghi an yakuna hadha 'ala qawl Muhammad' implying preference for Muhammad's view.
3
Identify the second condition
The principal (original witness) must not forbid the branches after imposing. If the principals are present and forbid, the prohibition is valid according to most scholars.
The text cites al-Khulasah quoting al-Jami' al-Kabir.
4
Note the Christian convert case
If a Christian makes someone a witness on his testimony then converts to Islam, the branch cannot testify. The text gives two interpretations, the first being more apparent.
This illustrates an additional condition related to the original witness's status.
5
Compare the practical effect
Under Muhammad's view, rejection is final; under the Sahibayn, rejection can be overlooked and the branch can still testify later.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the ability to testify after rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات يصير حتى يقبل التحميل فلو أشهده عليها فقال لا أقبل فإنه لا شاهداً لو شهد بعد ذلك لا تقبل كما في القنية، وينبغي أن يكون هذا على قول محمد من أنه توكيل وللوكيل أن لا يقبل، وأما على قولهما من أنه تحميل فلا يبطل بالرد لأن من حمل غيره شهادة لم تبطل بالرد. الثاني أن لا ينهاه الأصيل بعد التحميل عنها لما في الخلاصة معزياً إلى الجامع الكبير : لو حضر الأصلان ونهيا الفروع عن الشهادة صح النهي عند عامة المشايخ، وقال بعضهم لا يصح والأول أظهر ا هـ . وفي النوازل : النصراني إذا أشهد على شهادته ثم أسلم لم يجز أن يشهد على شهادته ا هـ. ويحتمل أن يكون مراده أنه أشهد نصرانياً مثله، ويحتمل أنه أشهد مسلماً والأول أظهر كما لا يخفى وقيد بالشهادة عليها لأن الشهادة بقضاء القاضي صحيحة وإن لم يشهدهما القاضي عليه لكن ذكر في الخلاصة خلافاً بين أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف فيما إذا سمعاه في غير مجلس القضاء فجوزه أبو حنيفة وهو الأقبس ومنعه أبو وهو الأحوط ا هـ . وجزم بالجواز في المعراج معللاً بأن القضاء حجة ملزمة ومن سمع الحجة حل له أن يشهد بها وفي شرح أدب القضاء للصدر من الباب الأربعين ضاع سجل من ديوان القاضي فشهد كاتباه عنده أنه أمضى ذلك فإن القاضي يقبله، ولو ضاع إقرار رجل فشهد كاتباه عنده بأنه أقر عنده يقضي بشهادتهما، ولو ضاع محضر من ديوانه فيه شهادة شهود بحق لا يذكره القاضي فشهدا عنده أن الشهود شهدوا عنده بكذا لا يقبلها القاضي ولا ينفذه لأن الشهود لم يحملاهما ولا بد منه وتمامه فيه . ثم اعلم أن القضاء بشهادة الفروع عندهما وعند محمد بشهادة الكل؛ كذا في الخزانة . ولو قال المؤلف كما في الهداية ما لم يشهد عليها لكان أولى من قوله «عليه» لما في الخزانة : لو قال اشهد علي بكذا أو اشهد علي ما شهدت به كان باطلاً، ولا بد أن يقول اشهد على شهادتي إلى آخره. اهـ. يوسف اهـ. وانظر كلام الفتح فإنه يفيد ذلك أيضاً قوله : (كان أبو حنيفة إلى قوله وهو المختار) قال الرملي : هنا حذف ولعله بعد قولها أنها فلانة وعندهما يكتفى بشهادة اثنين أنها فلانة. ثم راجعت النوازل فوجدتها كما أصلحتها ثم قال : وكان أبو يوسف وأبوك يقولان يجوز إذا شهد عنده عدلان أنها فلانة قوله : ( وفيه نظر لأنها لا تكون ملزمة لا بالقضاء أي لا تكون ملزمة للخصم، والظاهر أن مراد المحقق أنها ملزمة للقاضي الحكم بها إذ لا يجوز له تأخير الحكم بها إلا في مواضع تقدمت في القضاء، وما ذكر المحقق صرح به في النهاية كما ذكره في الدر المختار ثم قال : ويخالفه تصوير صدر الشريعة وغيره اهـ وعبارة الصدر : سمع رجل أداء الشهادة عند القاضي لم شهادته اهـ قوله : (وترك المؤلف قيدين آخرين لا يخفى أنه ليس مراده هنا بيان أحكام الشهادة على الشهادة حتى يستوفي شروطها وإنما ذلك له باب مخصوص سيأتي، ومراده هنا إظهار الفرق بينها وبين غيرها من المسوغات والمرئيات في اشتراط الإشهاد وعدمه فتدبر . يسع له أن يشهد على
Book of Testimonies: It becomes... until he accepts the imposition. So if he made him witness upon it and he said 'I do not accept', then he is not a witness; if he testifies after that, it is not accepted as in al-Qunyah. And it should be that this is according to the saying of Muhammad that it is agency (tawkeel) and the agent may not accept. As for according to the saying of the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) that it is imposition (tahmeel), it is not invalidated by rejection because the one who imposes testimony on another does not become invalid by rejection. The second condition: that the principal does not forbid the branches after the imposition. As in al-Khulasah quoting al-Jami' al-Kabir: If the two principals are present and forbid the branches from testifying, the prohibition is valid according to general mashayikh, and some said it is not valid, and the first is more apparent. And in al-Nawazil: If a Christian makes someone witness upon his testimony then becomes Muslim, it is not permissible for him to testify upon his testimony. It is possible that his intent is that he made a Christian like him a witness, and it is possible that he made a Muslim a witness, and the first is more apparent as is not hidden. And it is restricted to testimony upon it because testimony on a judge's judgment is valid even if the judge did not make them witnesses upon it, but in al-Khulasah a difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is mentioned regarding when they heard it outside the court: Abu Hanifa permitted it and it is the stronger, and Abu Yusuf forbade it and it is the more precautionary. And permissibility is affirmed in al-Mi'raj with the reasoning that judgment is a binding proof, and whoever hears the proof may testify by it. And in Sharh Adab al-Qada' by al-Sadr, from the fortieth chapter: If a record from the judge's court is lost, and his two scribes testify before him that he passed it, then the judge accepts it. And if a man's confession is lost, and his two scribes testify before him that he confessed before him, he judges by their testimony. And if a record from his court containing the testimony of witnesses to a right that the judge does not remember is lost, and they testify before him that the witnesses testified before him of such-and-such, the judge does not accept it and does not execute it because the witnesses did not impose upon them [the scribes] and it is necessary. And its completion is in it. Then know that judgment by the testimony of branches according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and according to Muhammad is judgment by the testimony of all? Actually ambiguous. And if the author said as in al-Hidayah 'ma lam yashhad alayha' it would be better than his statement 'alayh' because in al-Khizanah: If he said 'I bear witness against you of such-and-such' or 'I bear witness against you of what I testified' it is invalid, and he must say 'I bear witness upon my testimony' etc. End. Yusuf? End. And look at the words of al-Fath, for it benefits that as well. His statement: 'Abu Hanifa used to... until his statement: and it is the chosen one.' Al-Ramli said: There is an omission here, and perhaps after his statement that she is so-and-so, and according to the two, the testimony of two men that she is so-and-so suffices. Then I checked al-Nawazil and found it as I corrected it. Then he said: And Abu Yusuf and your father used to say it is permissible if two just men testify before him that she is so-and-so. His statement: 'And there is consideration because it is not binding...' by judgment, meaning it is not binding upon the opponent. And the apparent meaning of the investigator is that it is binding upon the judge to rule by it, because he may not delay ruling by it except in places that preceded in the chapter on judgment. And what the investigator mentioned is stated in al-Nihayah as mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Then he said: And it is contradicted by the depiction of Sadr al-Shariah and others. End. And the expression of al-Sadr: A man heard the performance of testimony before the judge... his testimony? End. His statement: 'And the author left out two other conditions.' It is not hidden that his intent here is not to explain the rulings of testimony on testimony until he exhausts its conditions, but rather that has a specific chapter to come. His intent here is to show the difference between it and other justifications and perceptions in the requirement of making witnesses and its absence, so ponder. It is permissible for him to testify upon...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the rules of testimony on testimony (الشهادة على الشهادة). The author first states a condition: that the branch witness must accept the imposition of testimony. If the original witness says 'I do not accept,' then the branch cannot testify later. He attributes this to Imam Muhammad's view that making someone a witness is like agency (tawkeel), and the agent may reject. According to the other two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it is imposition (tahmeel), and rejection does not invalidate it. Then a second condition: the principal (original witness) must not forbid the branch after imposing. The author cites al-Khulasah that if the two principals forbid the branches, the prohibition is valid according to most scholars. Then a case: a Christian who takes testimony and then converts; he cannot testify on that testimony. The author notes two interpretations. Then a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf about hearing a judgment outside court: Abu Hanifa allows testifying on it, Abu Yusuf does not. Then the author discusses cases of lost records and scribes testifying. Finally, he corrects the author of Kanz's wording: it should be 'ashhad 'ala shahadati' not 'ashhad 'alayh'. He also addresses the phrase 'was the testimony of two men that she is so-and-so' and discusses whether the judge is bound to accept it. The overall purpose is to differentiate testimony on testimony from other justifications.

Key Terms
تحميل (tahmeel): Imposing the testimony on someone as a witness, so the original witness makes the branch a witness on his testimony without giving him agency.
توكيل (tawkeel): Making someone a witness by giving him agency to testify on one's behalf.
الأصيل (al-aseel): The original witness who is the principal on whose testimony the branch testifies.
الفرع (al-far'): The branch witness who testifies on behalf of the original witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which Hanafi scholar views the act of making someone a witness on testimony as tawkeel (agency)?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Muhammad
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The branch's acceptance is a condition for testimony on testimony according to the stronger Hanafi view (Muhammad), based on the nature of agency.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony on Judgment and Scribe Testimony: Further Details
Explaining testimony on judgment and scribe testimony cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on hearing a judgment outside court and apply the rules for scribe testimony when records are lost.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling is a binding proof; one who hears it may testify, but Abu Yusuf insists on hearing it in court.

Beyond the basic conditions, this lesson covers the permissibility of testifying on a judge's judgment heard outside court, a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and cases where court records are lost and scribes must testify.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule on judgment testimony
The text says testimony on a judge's judgment is valid even if the judge did not specifically make them witnesses upon it.
This is because the judgment itself is a binding proof.
2
Note the dispute on hearing outside court
Abu Hanifa permits testifying on a judgment heard outside the courtroom; Abu Yusuf forbids it. The text calls Abu Hanifa's view 'the stronger' and Abu Yusuf's 'the more precautionary'.
This dispute is from al-Khulasah.
3
Apply to scribe testimony cases
If court records are lost, scribes (or others who witnessed the process) can testify: e.g., if a record of a judgment is lost, the judge can accept the scribes' testimony. But if a record of witnesses' testimony is lost and the judge does not remember, the scribes cannot testify because the witnesses did not impose upon them.
The key distinction is whether the scribes were personally tasked with carrying the testimony.
4
Recognize the correction of wording
The author criticizes the original author of Kanz for using 'ashhad 'alayh' instead of 'ashhad 'ala shahadati'. The correct phrasing is essential for validity.
The phrase 'ashhad 'ala shahadati' means 'I bear witness upon my testimony'.
5
Relate to the broader purpose
The passage aims to distinguish testimony on testimony from other justifications (like testimony on judgment) in terms of conditions.
The author says 'his intent here is to show the difference'.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the rules of testimony on testimony (الشهادة على الشهادة). The author first states a condition: that the branch witness must accept the imposition of testimony. If the original witness says 'I do not accept,' then the branch cannot testify later. He attributes this to Imam Muhammad's view that making someone a witness is like agency (tawkeel), and the agent may reject. According to the other two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it is imposition (tahmeel), and rejection does not invalidate it. Then a second condition: the principal (original witness) must not forbid the branch after imposing. The author cites al-Khulasah that if the two principals forbid the branches, the prohibition is valid according to most scholars. Then a case: a Christian who takes testimony and then converts; he cannot testify on that testimony. The author notes two interpretations. Then a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf about hearing a judgment outside court: Abu Hanifa allows testifying on it, Abu Yusuf does not. Then the author discusses cases of lost records and scribes testifying. Finally, he corrects the author of Kanz's wording: it should be 'ashhad 'ala shahadati' not 'ashhad 'alayh'. He also addresses the phrase 'was the testimony of two men that she is so-and-so' and discusses whether the judge is bound to accept it. The overall purpose is to differentiate testimony on testimony from other justifications.

Key Terms
تحميل (tahmeel): Imposing the testimony on someone as a witness, so the original witness makes the branch a witness on his testimony without giving him agency.
القضاء (al-qada): Judgment or judicial decision.
الكاتب (al-katib): Court scribe who records proceedings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a record of witnesses' testimony is lost and the judge does not remember it, can the scribes testify about it?
A) Yes, because they saw the witnesses.
B) No, because the witnesses did not impose upon them.
C) Only if the judge asks them.
D) Yes, but only one scribe is needed.

Key Takeaway
Testimony on a judgment is valid even if heard outside court (Abu Hanifa's view), but scribes can only testify to what they were personally tasked to carry.

Reliance on Writing Without Memory: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the ruling that a witness, judge, or narrator cannot act based on his writing unless he remembers, with disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on relying on one's own handwriting without memory, identify the mufta bihi view, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving witnesses, judges, and narrators.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi allows reliance on one's own writing if certain of the handwriting—an ease for people.

When a witness, judge, or narrator finds his own written record but cannot remember the content, can he act on it? The Hanafi imams gave three different answers. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and identifies the operative opinion for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common legal problem
Recognize that the issue is whether a person (witness, judge, or narrator) may rely on his own writing if he does not recall what he wrote.
The writing includes handwritten notes, court registers, or hadith notebooks.
2
State Abu Hanifa's strict position
Abu Hanifa requires the person to independently remember the content (subject matter, date, amount, etc.) before acting. Without memory, acting on writing is like false testimony.
He required memory from the time of hearing/seeing to the time of acting.
3
State Muhammad's permissive position
Muhammad allows reliance on writing if one is certain that the writing is his own, even without memory. This is for ease (tawsi'ah).
His view is the mufta bihi.
4
State Abu Yusuf's intermediate position
Abu Yusuf allows reliance for the judge and narrator but not for the witness. A witness must still remember; a judge may rely on his own record.
This is a compromise between strictness and ease.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical application
Identify that later authorities (al-Halwani, al-Bazzaziyyah) rule by Muhammad's view. Then apply: a witness, judge, or narrator may act on his writing if certain it is his, without needing to recall the content.
The judge may also ask whether testimony is based on knowledge or writing.

Source Text

وإن شهد فهو شاهد زور؛ كذا في الخلاصة ولا يكفي تذكر مجلس الشهادة. وفي الملتقط: وعلى الشاهد أن يشهد وإن لم يعرف مكان الشهادة ووقتها اهـ. وجوز محمد للكل الاعتماد على الكتاب إذا تيقن أنه خطه وإن لم يتذكر يفتى بقول محمد. وهكذا في الأجناس كذا في الخلاصة . وجزم في البزازية بأنه يفتى بقول محمد. وفي المبتغى بالغين المعجمة من وجد خطه وعرفه ونسي الشهادة وسعه أن يشهد إذا كان في حوزه وبه نأخذ ا هـ . وعزاه في فيها. ويتفرع على الاختلاف السابق مسائل حاصلها أيجوز الاعتماد من على غير الحفظ من إخبار مخبر بقضاء أو شهادة أو رواية أم لا: الأولى لو نسي القاضي قضاءه أنه كان شاهداً. ولم يكن له سجل فشهدا عنده أنه قضى بكذا. الثانية أخبره قوم يثق . بهم حديثاً من غيره ثم نسي راوي الأصل فسمعه ممن روى عنه. ثم اعلم أن الشاهد سمع الثالثة إذا اعتمد على خطه على القول المفتى به وشهد وقلنا بقبوله فللقاضي أن يسأله هل يشهد عن علم أم عن الخط؛ إن قال عن علم قبله، وإن قال عن الخط لا كما في البزازية. وفي المعراج : وعلى الاختلاف لو سمع من غيره حديثاً ثم نسي الأصل الرواية فعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لا يعمل به وعند محمد يعمل به . وعلى هذا المسائل التي اختلف فيها أبو يوسف ومحمد في الرواية في الجامع الصغير وهي ثلاث سمعها محمد من أبي يوسف ثم نسي أبو يوسف الرواية فكان لا يعتمد على رواية محمد وهو لا يدع الرواية ا هـ. وهي ست لا ثلاث His statement: 'And a witness, judge, and narrator should not act on the basis of writing unless they remember.' Meaning: It is not permissible for a witness, when he sees his handwriting, to testify until he remembers. Likewise, a judge when he finds in his register a written testimony of witnesses and does not remember, and nor for a narrator to narrate relying on what is in his book unless he remembers. And this is the saying of the Imam (Abu Hanifa). And the object of 'remember' is omitted, and the intent is generalization; so it is necessary according to him for the witness to remember the incident, date, amount of property and its description, so that if he does not remember anything of it but is certain that it is his handwriting and seal, he should not testify, and if he testifies, he is a false witness. Thus in al-Khulasah. And remembering the session of testimony is not sufficient. In al-Multaqat: It is obligatory for the witness to testify even if he does not know the place and time of the testimony. End. And Muhammad permitted for everyone to rely on the writing if he is certain it is his handwriting, even if he does not remember, as an ease for people. And Abu Yusuf permitted it for the narrator and the judge but not for the witness. In al-Khulasah, Abu Hanifa was strict in all, such that his narration of reports is few; despite his extensive hearing, he narrated only from 1200 men, but he required memory from the time of hearing to the time of narration. The place of disagreement is concerning the judge when he finds his own written judgment. And they all agreed that a judge does not act on what he finds in the register of another judge, even if sealed. Thus in al-Khulasah. And Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani said: Fatwa should be given according to Muhammad's view. And thus in al-Ajnas, as in al-Khulasah. And it is asserted in al-Bazzaziyyah that one gives fatwa according to Muhammad. In al-Mubtagha: One who finds his handwriting and recognizes it but forgot the testimony may testify if it is in his possession, and we take this. End. And he attributed it in al-Bazzaziyyah to al-Nawazil. And his statement 'should not act' indicates that if a witness writes his testimony in a copy and reads it for the purpose of retention, it is accepted because he did not rely on his handwriting. And a chapter on this was included in al-Sirajiyyah, saying 'Chapter on testimony by copy' etc. And branching from the previous difference are issues: The gist is: Is it permissible to rely on other than memory from the report of an informer about a judgment, testimony, or narration? First: If a judge forgets his own judgment that he was a witness, and he has no record, so two [witnesses] testify before him that he judged such-and-such. Second: A group he trusts inform him of a hadith from someone else; then the original narrator forgets, and he hears it from the one who narrated from him. Third: If he relies on his handwriting according to the mufta bihi view and testifies, and we say it is accepted, then the judge may ask him: Do you testify based on knowledge or based on writing? If he says based on knowledge, he accepts it; if he says based on writing, he does not. As in al-Bazzaziyyah. In al-Mi'raj: And according to the difference, if one hears a hadith from someone else then the original narrator forgets the narration, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is not acted upon, but according to Muhammad it is acted upon. And upon this are the issues about which Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differed in narration in al-Jami' al-Saghir: three issues which Muhammad heard from Abu Yusuf, then Abu Yusuf forgot the narration, so he did not rely on Muhammad's narration but he did not abandon the narration. End. They are six, not three, as we have transmitted, explained in our commentary on al-Manar. And they were refuted in Fath al-Qadir here and in the Book of Prayer that the story between the two shaykhs indicates that it is from the chapter of the original disbelieving the branch, and there is no disagreement among them.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on whether a witness, judge, or narrator can rely on their own writing without remembering the content. The text begins by explaining the statement in the matn: a witness should not testify based on his handwriting unless he remembers; similarly a judge should not rely on a written testimony in his register without memory; and a narrator should not narrate from his book without memory. This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. For the witness, he must remember the incident, date, amount, and description of the property. If he does not remember but is certain of his handwriting and seal, he should not testify; if he does, he is a false witness. Remembering the session is not sufficient. However, al-Multaqat states that the witness must testify even if he does not know the place and time. Then the author presents the views of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf: Muhammad allows everyone to rely on the writing if certain it is his handwriting, as an ease; Abu Yusuf allows it for the narrator and judge but not for the witness. The text notes that Abu Hanifa was strict in all matters, even requiring memory from time of hearing to time of narration, which is why his narrated reports are few. The disagreement is specifically about the judge finding his own written judgment. They all agree that a judge cannot act on a record from another judge's register. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is that of Muhammad, as stated by Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani, and confirmed in al-Ajnas and al-Bazzaziyyah. Then the author clarifies that the statement 'should not act' does not apply to a witness who writes his testimony in a copy for retention and reads it; that is permissible because he is not solely relying on the handwriting. Then the author lists derived issues: First: if a judge forgets his own judgment and two witnesses testify before him that he judged that way. Second: if a trusted group reports a hadith to someone, then the original narrator forgets, and the intermediate hears it from them. Third: if one relies on his handwriting on the mufta bihi view, the judge may ask whether his testimony is based on knowledge or on writing; only if based on knowledge is it accepted. The passage concludes with reference to a dispute in al-Jami' al-Saghir between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad about whether to act on a narration when the original narrator forgets. The author claims there are six issues, not three, and discusses Fath al-Qadir's refutation, saying the story is about the original disbelieving the branch, not about disagreement on the principle.

Key Terms
الاعتماد على الخط (al-i'timad 'ala al-khatt): Relying on one's own handwriting without independently remembering the content of what was written.
شاهد زور (shahid zoor): A false witness who testifies without proper knowledge, which is a major sin according to Abu Hanifa if done relying on writing without memory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No one may rely on handwriting without memory.
B) Only a judge may rely on handwriting without memory.
C) Anyone may rely on his handwriting if he is certain it is his handwriting, even without memory.
D) Only a witness may rely on handwriting without memory.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the operative opinion permits reliance on one's own writing if one is certain of the handwriting, as an ease for people.
