Reliance on Handwriting Without Memory
Explains the ruling on witnesses, judges, and narrators relying on their own writing without recollection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on testifying from one's own writing without memory and identify the mufta bihi opinion.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa rejects testimony from writing without memory; Muhammad permits it for practical ease.

A witness sees his own signature on a document but cannot recall the incident. May he still testify? This question splits the three early Hanafi imams. We explore the ruling, its rationale, and which opinion is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the strict view: the witness, judge, or narrator must actually remember the event; seeing the writing alone is insufficient. Without memory, testifying is false testimony.
Abu Hanifa requires detailed recall of the incident, date, amount, etc.
2
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi views
Abu Yusuf permits reliance on writing for narrators and judges but not witnesses. Muhammad permits it for all if certain the writing is theirs, for convenience.
Muhammad's view is the broadest.
3
Analyze the basis of each view
Abu Hanifa emphasizes certainty and avoiding falsehood. Muhammad considers the practical need to ease affairs. Abu Yusuf balances both.
No explicit Quranic or hadith proof is cited; it is a matter of istihsan and maslaha.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
Later scholars like al-Hulwani and the author of al-Bazzaziyya rule that fatwa is given according to Muhammad's view, though some (e.g., in Fath al-Qadir) find it problematic.
The dispute in al-Jami' al-Saghir is argued to be about different grounds (takhdhib), not mere forgetfulness.

كما نقلناها مبينة في شرحنا على المنار، وتعقبهم في فتح القدير هنا وفي كتاب الصلاة بأن الحكاية التي جرت بين الشيخين تفيد أنه من باب تكذيب الأصل الفرع ولا خلاف عندهم في بطلان الرواية لا أنه من باب النسيان فاعتماد المشايخ على قول محمد مشكل .
He (the author) said: 'A witness, judge, and narrator do not act by the writing if they do not remember.' That is, it is not permissible for a witness when he sees his own handwriting to testify until he remembers; similarly the judge if he finds in his register written testimony and does not remember; nor the narrator to narrate relying on his book unless he remembers. This is the statement of Abu Hanifa. He requires remembering the incident, date, amount, and description. If he testifies without memory, he is a false witness. Muhammad permitted everyone to rely on the writing if certain it is his line, even without memory, for ease. Abu Yusuf permitted it for the narrator and judge but not the witness. Abu Hanifa was strict in all. Fatwa is given according to Muhammad's opinion, though some scholars find this problematic.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage addresses a key principle in testimony and narration: the necessity of memory. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that a witness, judge, or narrator 'does not act by the writing' if they do not remember. The commentator, Imam Ibn Nujaym, explains that according to Abu Hanifa, it is not permissible for a witness to testify based solely on seeing his own handwriting; he must actually remember the event. Similarly, a judge cannot rely on a written record in his register without recollection, and a hadith narrator cannot narrate from his notes without memory. Abu Hanifa is the strictest, requiring detailed memory of the incident, date, and amount. Abu Yusuf allows reliance for narrators and judges but not witnesses, while Muhammad allows it for all if they are certain the writing is theirs, for the sake of ease. The commentator notes that fatwa is often given according to Muhammad's view, though some later scholars (like in Fath al-Qadir) criticize this as problematic because the case between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir was about the original considering the branch a liar, not mere forgetfulness. This intra-madhhab dispute shows the tension between strict evidentiary standards and practical convenience.

Key Terms
شاهد (shahid): A witness who testifies in court about a fact.
خط (khatt): Handwriting; the written record of testimony or judgment.
تذكر (tadhakkur): Remembrance; mental recall of the event or fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, on what condition may a witness testify from his own handwriting without memory?
A) He must also have the original contract.
B) He must be certain the writing is his.
C) He must be a judge or narrator, not a witness.
D) He must first consult the court.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has three positions on testifying from handwriting without memory; the relied-upon opinion (Muhammad's) permits it for all if certain of the writing, balancing evidentiary rigor with practical needs.

Testimony by Hearsay in Exceptional Cases
List of exceptions to hearsay rule with procedural expansions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six exceptional matters where hearsay testimony is permitted in Hanafi fiqh and apply the procedural rules for claims of lineage and naming in lawsuits.

Core Idea
Hearsay testimony is allowed in six specific matters to prevent hardship, and claims of lineage require a property claim except for father and son.

The general rule in Islamic evidence law is that testimony must be based on direct observation. However, strict adherence would cause great difficulty in matters that are not typically witnessed by the public—such as lineage, death, marriage, cohabitation, the appointment of a judge, and the founding of an endowment. The Hanafi school, through the principle of istihsan, grants an exception for these six cases. This lesson examines those exceptions, the justification of istihsan, and then explores the detailed fiqh of how such hearsay testimony is applied in court, including conditions for acceptance and the issue of naming parties.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the General Prohibition
Understand that the default rule is that testimony requires direct observation (mushahada) or hearing (sama'). The text begins by affirming that a witness should not testify to what he has not witnessed.
This is based on the Quranic principle of testifying with knowledge.
2
Memorize the Six Exceptions
The text lists six matters: lineage (nasab), death (mawt), marriage (nikah), cohabitation (dukhul), judge's authority (wilaya al-qadi), and original endowment (asl al-waqf). These are the only cases where hearsay testimony is accepted.
Some later scholars added others, but these six are the standard from the matn.
3
Analyze the Rationale: Istihsan
The exception is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent hardship (haraj) and avoid suspension of legal rulings (ta'til al-ahkam). These matters are often known only by a few people, so requiring direct testimony would make it impossible to prove them.
Istihsan is a valid source of law in the Hanafi school.
4
Examine the Application: Conditions for Lineage Claims
The text from al-Bazzaziyya clarifies that when testifying to lineage, the court does not accept the testimony until there is a claim of property (mal), except for the relationship of father and son. Also, in claims of unclehood, the specific type (paternal, maternal, or both) must be stated.
This prevents frivolous claims of lineage without material interest.
5
Understand the Rule on Naming Parties
The text concludes that naming the names of parties is not a condition for the validity of a lawsuit or testimony. This is the relied-upon view of later Hanafi scholars.
This facilitates proceedings when names are unknown or disputed.

قال فالحاصل أنه في دعوى الفعل والشهادة على الفعل هل تشترط تسمية الفاعل؟ فيه اختلاف المشايخ رحمهم الله وأدلة الكتب فيها متعارضة، ثم ذكر مسائل وقال : وهذه المسائل كلها تدل على أن تسمية الفاعل ليست بشرط لصحة الدعوى والشهادة فتأمل عند الفتوى . قوله : (وحاصل ما ينفعنا هنا الخ الأنفع ما في شرح الوهبانية عن العمادية من قوله حتى لو
He (the author) said: 'And he does not testify to what he has not witnessed except in lineage, death, marriage, entry (consummation), the judge's authority, and the original endowment. So he may testify to them if he is informed by someone he trusts, by istihsan, to prevent hardship and suspension of rulings, since only special people attend those. The meaning: It is not permissible for him to testify to something he has not ascertained by sight or hearing except in these. As for lineage, it means connection by kinship. It is established that testimony by hearsay is permissible in these matters. In al-Bazzaziyya: In a claim of unclehood, one must specify if he is the uncle through the mother or father or both, and must state that he is his heir with no other heir. If he proves that or that he is the brother of the deceased from both parents and they do not know of another heir, he is awarded the property. Names are not required in lawsuits... [then discussion about claims of brotherhood, paternity, etc.] The conclusion beneficial here is that when witnesses testify to lineage, the judge does not accept them nor rule until after a claim of property, except in the case of father and son. He said: 'And naming of names is not required in lawsuits.' Al-Ramli said: ... the conclusion is that naming the doer is not a condition for the validity of a lawsuit and testimony, so reflect when giving fatwa. The beneficial point is what is in Sharh al-Wahbaniyya... until even if...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage addresses the important exception to the general rule that testimony must be based on direct observation. The base text lists six matters where testimony by hearsay (shahada bi al-tasamu') is allowed: lineage, death, marriage, cohabitation (dakhul), the authority of a judge (wilaya al-qadi), and the original endowment (asl al-waqf). The commentator explains that this is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid hardship and to prevent the nullification of legal rulings, since these matters are often witnessed only by a few people. The text then delves into detailed fiqh rulings on claims of lineage, including the need for a property claim to be attached in certain cases. It also discusses the principle that naming the names of parties is not required in lawsuits, citing the view of later scholars. The passage thus provides practical guidance for judges and witnesses in dealing with hearsay evidence and procedural matters.

Key Terms
تسامع (tasamu'): Hearsay; testimony based on common knowledge or information from others rather than direct observation.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from analogy for a stronger reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only in lineage and death.
B) In lineage, death, marriage, cohabitation, judge's authority, and original endowment.
C) In all contractual matters.
D) Only in judicial rulings.

Key Takeaway
Hearsay testimony is generally prohibited but allowed in six specific cases by istihsan to prevent hardship; claims of lineage require a property claim except for father and son.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
10 min
Hearsay Testimony in Hanafi Fiqh: Exceptions for Lineage and Five Matters
Ruling on permissible hearsay testimony and conditions for lineage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five matters where hearsay testimony is permitted, explain the rationale (istihsan), and apply the conditions for accepting hearsay testimony on lineage, including the property claim requirement.

Core Idea
Testimony by hearsay is permitted in five matters to avoid hardship, but lineage requires a property claim except for father-son.

In Hanafi fiqh, the default rule is that testimony must be based on direct observation (mu'ayana). However, the jurists recognized that for certain private or public-interest matters, requiring direct witnesses would cause hardship and potentially suspend important legal rulings. Therefore, they permitted testimony based on reliable hearsay (tasammu') in five specific matters, using the principle of istihsan (juristic preference). This lesson examines those exceptions, focusing particularly on lineage, which has additional conditions rooted in the need to protect the rights of absent parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule and Its Exception
First, note the default rule: one cannot testify except to what they directly witnessed. Then identify the five exceptions where hearsay is permitted: death, marriage, consummation, qadi's appointment, and origin of waqf. Understand the rationale: istihsan to remove hardship.
The exceptions are limited; hearsay is not generally permitted for other matters.
2
Analyze the Conditions for Lineage Testimony
Lineage hearsay is permissible, but the passage details that for claims of unclehood or brotherhood, the claimant must specify the type of relation and, crucially, must claim property (inheritance or maintenance) for the testimony to be accepted. Expand on why this requirement exists: to avoid imposing lineage on an absent person (the father) without a legal interest.
Property claim is essential except for direct father-son or son-father claims.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Position
Recognize that there is a difference within the Hanafi school: Imam Muhammad required widespread fame for lineage hearsay, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allowed it with two just witnesses. The fatwa is according to the Sahibayn.
The text mentions 'qawlahuma' as the mufta bihi.
4
Apply the Rule to a Scenario
Consider a case where two reliable witnesses testify that a deceased person's uncle is a certain man. According to the text, the qadi will only accept this if the uncle also claims property. If the uncle merely claims the relationship, it is not accepted. Test your understanding by deriving the ruling for a sibling claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات
والموت والنكاح والدخول وولاية القاضي وأصل الوقف فله أن يشهد بها إذا أخبره بها من يثق به استحساناً دفعاً للحرج وتعطيل الأحكام إذ لا يحضرها إلا الخواص. والمراد لا يحل له أن يشهد بشيء لم يقطع به من جهة المعاينة بالعين أو السماع إلا في كذا، أما النسب فمن نسبته إلى أبيه نسباً من باب طلب عزوته إليه وانتسب إليه اعتزى ثم استعمل النسب وهو المصدر في مطلق الوصلة بالقرابة فيقال بينهما نسب أي قرابة وسواء جاز بينهما التناكح أم لا وجمعه أنساب وتمامه في المصباح. وأما ما يتعلق به من الأحكام هنا فأفاد أنه تجوز الشهادة فيه بالتسامع. وفي البزازية من الدعوى العاشر في النسب : وفي دعوى العمومة لا بد أن يفسر أنه عمه لأمة أو لأبيه أو لهما، ويشترط أيضاً أن يقول هو وارثه لا وارث له غيره فإن برهن على ذلك أو على أنه أخو الميت لأبويه لا يعلمون أن له وارثاً غيره يحكم له بالمال. ولا يشترط ذكر الأسماء في الأقضية إلى أن قال : ادعى على آخر أنه أخوه لأبيه ادعى إرثاً أو نفقة وبرهن تقبل ويكون قضاء على الغائب أيضاً حتى لو حضر الأب وأنكر لا يقبل ولا يحتاج إلى إعادة البينة لأنه لا يتوصل إليه إلا بإثبات الحق على الغائب، وإن لم يدع مالاً بل ادعى الأخوة المجردة لا تقبل لأن هذا في الحقيقة إثبات البنوة على الأب المدعى عليه والخصم فيه هو الأب لا الأخ. وكذا ادعى فإن لو ادعى أنه ابن ابنه أو أبو أبيه والابن والأب غائب أو ميت لا يصح ما لم يدع مالاً، مالاً فالحكم على الحاضر والغائب جميعاً بخلاف ما إذا ادعى على رجل أنه أبوه أو ابنه أو على امرأة أنها زوجته أو ادعت عليه أنه زوجها أو ادعى العبد على عربي أنه مولاه عتاقة أو ادعى عربي على أخر أنه معتقة أو ادعت على رجل أنها أمته أو كان الدعوى في ولاء الموالاة وأنكر المدعى عليه فبرهن المدعي على ما قال يقبل ادعى به حقاً أو لا، بخلاف دعوى الأخوة لأنه دعوى الغير ألا : تری أنه لو أقر أنه أبوه أو ابنه أو زوجه أو زوجته صح أو بأنه أخوه لا لكونه حمل النسب على الغير وتمامه فيها. وحاصل ما ينفعنا هنا أن الشهود إذا شهدوا بنسب فإن القاضي لا يقبلهم ولا يحكم به إلا بعد دعوى مال إلا في الأب والابن.
The Book of Testimonies. And [testimony on] death, marriage, entry, the appointment of the qadi, and the origin of the endowment: it is permissible for him to testify based on them if someone he trusts informs him, by way of istihsan, to remove hardship and avoid suspension of rulings, since only elites attend these [matters]. And the meaning is: it is not permissible for him to testify to something he is not certain of through direct eye-witnessing or hearing except in these [cases]. As for lineage: it is from his attribution to his father, as a matter of seeking connection to him, and he is attributed to him. Then lineage is used as a source for absolute connection by kinship; it is said 'between them is lineage' meaning kinship, whether marriage between them is permissible or not; its plural is 'ansab'. And its completion is in al-Misbah. And as for the rulings related to it here, it indicates that testimony by hearsay is permissible in it. And in al-Bazzaziyya, from the tenth [chapter] on claims in lineage: and in a claim of paternal unclehood, he must explain whether he is his uncle through his mother, his father, or both. And it is also required that he say 'he is his heir and he has no other heir'. If he proves that, or that he is the brother of the deceased from both parents, and they do not know that he has any other heir, then he is judged to have the property. And it is not a condition to mention names in the judicial decisions, until he said: If someone claims against another that he is his paternal brother, claiming inheritance or maintenance, and he proves it, it is accepted and it is a judgment against an absent person as well, so that even if the father appears and denies, it is not accepted, and it is not necessary to repeat the evidence because he cannot achieve it except by establishing the right against the absent. But if he does not claim property but merely claims brotherhood, it is not accepted, because this is in reality establishing paternity against the claimed father, and the opponent in it is the father, not the brother. Similarly, if he claims that he is his son's son or his father's father, and the son and father are absent or dead, it is not valid unless he claims property. Then the judgment is against both the present and the absent, unlike if he claims against a man that he is his father or his son, or against a woman that she is his wife, or she claims against him that he is her husband, or a slave claims against an Arab that he is his master of emancipation, or an Arab claims against another that he is his freed slave, or a woman claims against a man that she is his slave-girl, or the claim is in patronage of clientage, and the defendant denies, then if the plaintiff proves what he said, it is accepted whether he claimed a right or not, unlike the claim of brotherhood because it is a claim against another. Do you not see that if he acknowledges that he is his father or his son or his husband or his wife, it is valid, but [if he acknowledges] that he is his brother, it is not, because it imposes lineage on another. And its completion is in it. And the summary of what benefits us here is that if witnesses testify regarding lineage, the qadi does not accept them nor judge by it except after a claim of property, except in the case of father and son.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, deals with the permissibility of testifying based on hearsay (al-tasammu') in five specific matters: death, marriage, consummation (or entry), the appointment of a qadi, and the origin of a waqf. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to remove hardship and prevent the suspension of legal rulings, since only a few people (the elite) are present at such events. The default rule is that one must testify only to what they have directly witnessed (by eye or ear), but these five exceptions allow testimony on the basis of reliable hearsay. The passage then focuses on lineage, explaining that testimony by hearsay is permitted for establishing lineage because of necessity, but with detailed conditions. For a claim of unclehood, one must specify whether the uncle is maternal or paternal. Furthermore, a claim of property (inheritance or maintenance) is necessary for the testimony to be accepted, except when the claimant is seeking to establish that the deceased is his father or son. The text explains that claiming mere brotherhood without a property claim is not accepted because it effectively imposes lineage on the alleged father, who is the real opponent. The summary states that witnesses testifying on lineage are only accepted if there is a claim of property, except for establishing fatherhood or sonship.

Key Terms
التسامع (al-tasammu'): Testimony based on hearsay, i.e., indirect knowledge received from others, as opposed to direct witnessing (mu'ayana).
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage or kinship relationship, particularly the attribution of a person to his father or family.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference based on equity, necessity, or public interest, used here to permit hearsay testimony in five matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the condition for accepting hearsay testimony on lineage (nasaab) in a court?
A) The witnesses must have directly witnessed the birth.
B) The claimant must also claim property (inheritance or maintenance) except for father-son claims.
C) The testimony must be from at least four male witnesses.
D) The lineage must be established by a written document.

Key Takeaway
Hearsay testimony is permitted in five matters by istihsan, but for lineage it requires a property claim unless the relationship is direct (father/son).

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Hearsay Testimony on Lineage
Ruling with proof on hearsay testimony for lineage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for accepting hearsay testimony on lineage in the Hanafi school and identify the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa's view and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Lineage can be proven by hearsay, but the witness must have certainty from a reliable source—either widespread report or two just witnesses.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a practical problem: how to prove lineage when direct observation of birth is impossible. The Hanafi school permits testimony based on hearsay (tasamu') but sets strict conditions to ensure reliability. The key issue is whether a group's widespread report suffices or whether two specific just witnesses are required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act being ruled upon is testifying based on hearsay (shahada bi al-tasamu') for the purpose of establishing lineage. Determine what type of ruling it is—permissibility or obligation? The text says 'it is permissible for him to testify' (وسعه أن يشهد), so the ruling is about the validity of the testimony, not its obligation.
In Hanafi fiqh, this is a ruling on the validity of the testimony, not on the hukm taklifi. The conditions determine whether the testimony is sahih (valid) or not.
2
Identify the three main positions
The passage presents three key positions: Imam Abu Hanifa's (widespread report from a group that cannot conspire), the Sahibayn's (two just witnesses informing the witness), and al-Khassaf's (additional condition of one year residence). Note also Abu Yusuf's variant of six months.
Be careful: the text uses 'عندهما' for the Sahibayn, and 'عند الإمام' for Abu Hanifa.
3
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
The text explicitly says 'الفتوى على قولهما' (the fatwa is on the opinion of the two). This means the operative ruling is the Sahibayn's requirement of two just witnesses. Al-Khassaf's conditions are additional but must be considered in practice.
Even though Imam Abu Hanifa's view is more lenient, the later madhhab adopted the stricter view for fatwa.
4
Apply to the scenario of a newcomer
The passage gives a concrete example: a man from another country claims lineage and resides with the witness for a long time. According to the Sahibayn, the witness cannot testify until two just witnesses from the man's country testify, or the lineage is well-known (shuhra).
Shuhra here means actual and customary notoriety, not mere rumor.
5
Recognize the limits of hearsay
The text warns that if the witness hears from someone who heard from the claimant, it is not acceptable even if widespread. However, if a large group testifies producing actual notoriety, or two just witnesses testify leading to legal notoriety, then testimony is permissible.
This shows that hearsay must ultimately be traceable to a reliable source, not just a chain of hearsay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمع من الناس أن هذا فلان بن فلان الفلاني وسعه أن يشهد به وإن لم يعاين الولادة على فراشه وطريقة معرفته أن ذلك جماعة لا يتصور تواطؤهم على الكذب عند الإمام، وعندهما إذا سمع من أخبره بذلك عدلان يكفي وذكر أن الفتوى على قولهما .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية عن المحيط : وإذا قدم عليه رجل من بلد آخر وانتسب إليه وأقام معه دهراً لم يسعه أن يشهد على نسبه حتى يشهد له رجلان من أهل بلده عدلان أو يكون النسب مشهوراً. وذكر الخصاف هذه المسألة وشرط لجواز الشهادة شرطين أن يشتهر الخبر والثاني أن يمكث فيهم سنة فإنه قال : لا يسعهم أن يشهدوا على نسبه حتى يقع معرفة ذلك في قلوبهم، وذلك بأن يقيم معهم سنة، وإن وقع في قلبه معرفة ذلك قبل مضي السنة لا يجوز أن يشهد وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه قدر ذلك بستة أشهر، والصحيح أنه إذا سمع من أهل بلده من رجلين عدلين حل له أداء الشهادة وإلا فلا. أما إذا سمع ذلك ممن سمع من المدعي لا يحل له أن يشهد وإن اشتهر ذلك فيما بين الناس لكنه إن شهد عنده جماعة حتى يقع الشهرة حقيقة وعرفاً وقع عنده أنه ثابت النسب من فلان أو شهد عنده عدلان حتى ثبت الاشتهار شرعاً حل له أن يشهد
If a person hears from the people that this is so-and-so son of so-and-so, it is permissible for him to testify to his lineage even if he did not witness his birth on his bed. His way of knowing is that a group of people whose collusion on a lie is inconceivable [convey it] according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to his two companions, if he hears from one who informed him of that by two just witnesses, it is sufficient, and it is mentioned that the fatwa is on the opinion of the two. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Muhit: If a man from another country comes to him and claims lineage to him and resides with him for a long time, he may not testify on his lineage until two just witnesses from the people of his country testify for him, or the lineage is well-known. And al-Khassaf mentioned this issue and stipulated two conditions for the permissibility of testimony: that the report is widespread, and the second that he resides with them for a year. He said: It is not permissible for them to testify on his lineage until that knowledge settles in their hearts, and that is by him residing with them for a year. And if knowledge of that settles in his heart before the passing of a year, it is not permissible for him to testify. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he estimated it at six months. The correct view is that if he hears from the people of his town from two just witnesses, it is permissible for him to perform testimony; otherwise not. But if he hears that from someone who heard from the claimant, it is not permissible for him to testify, even if that is widespread among the people. However, if a group testifies before him such that actual and customary notoriety occurs and it becomes established before him that the lineage is proven from so-and-so, or two just witnesses testify before him until the notoriety is proven legally, then it is permissible for him to testify.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the conditions for accepting hearsay testimony (الشهادة بالتسامع) specifically for establishing lineage (نسب). The Hanafi school recognizes that lineage can be proven through widespread reputation (شهرة) or through the testimony of two just witnesses, but the scholar who hears such information must have a certain level of certainty. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if a group of people whose collusion on a lie is unimaginable (جماعة لا يتصور تواطؤهم على الكذب) inform him, it is sufficient for him to testify. His two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) require two just witnesses (عدلان) informing him, and the fatwa is on their opinion. The passage then cites al-Khassaf's additional condition: the person whose lineage is being testified must have resided in the town for a full year so that knowledge settles in the hearts of the witnesses. Abu Yusuf is reported to have reduced this to six months, but the correct (صحيح) view is that if two just witnesses from his town testify, it is sufficient. Importantly, hearsay from someone who heard from the claimant is not acceptable, even if it is widespread. However, if actual notoriety (شهرة حقيقة وعرفاً) is established through many people or through two witnesses such that it becomes legally established, then testimony is permissible. This ruling shows the Hanafi caution in accepting lineage, balancing the need for proof with practical necessity.

Key Terms
شهادة بالتسامع (shahada bi al-tasamu'): Testimony based on hearsay or common knowledge without direct observation.
شهرة (shuhra): Widespread notoriety or common knowledge that constitutes proof for testimony.
عدل (adl): A just person whose testimony is acceptable; one who avoids major sins and upholds integrity.
تواطؤ (tawatu'): Collusion or conspiracy to lie; the phrase لا يتصور تواطؤهم على الكذب is a key evidenciary concept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi position regarding hearsay testimony on lineage?
A) Widespread report from a group whose collusion is impossible suffices.
B) Two just witnesses are required, and the fatwa is on the Sahibayn's opinion.
C) Residence of one year is sufficient regardless of witnesses.
D) A single reliable witness is enough.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, hearsay testimony on lineage is valid only if based on a source that provides certainty—either two just witnesses (mufta bihi) or actual widespread notoriety (shuhra).

Hearsay on Death and Conflicting Reports
Rules for a woman's remarriage based on hearsay and resolving conflicting reports on death and life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman acting on hearsay of her husband's death and apply the preference rules for conflicting death and life reports.

Core Idea
A woman may remarry on a single just witness's direct observation of death; conflicting reports are resolved by date or preference for death.

What happens when a woman hears that her husband has died far away, but she has no direct proof? The Hanafi school provides a practical path: she may remarry if a trustworthy person who actually saw the death or attended the funeral tells her. However, if reports conflict (some say dead, some alive), the jurists set clear rules for which report to follow. This lesson explores those rules and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what legal action is being considered (the woman's remarriage after hearing of husband's death) and the hukm level: it is permissible (mubah), not obligatory, but conditional.
The ruling is about permission, not obligation.
2
Examine the conditions for relying on hearsay
List the requirements for the informant: must be just (عدل) and must have directly observed the death (saw him dead or attended funeral). Hearsay from hearsay is not sufficient.
This restriction ensures reliability.
3
Apply the rules for conflicting reports
If one report says death and another says life, prefer the one with later date if dates are given. If no dates, prefer death report if the reporter observed directly. Also, a single death witness prevails over two life witnesses if the death witness observed directly.
This resolves uncertainty.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school requires two witnesses for death, while Hanafi accepts one just witness. The practical consequence is that a Hanafi woman may remarry sooner.
This is an area of ikhtilaf.
5
Consider the restriction for merchants
According to al-Mi'raj, hearsay is only accepted if the deceased is a scholar or worker; for a merchant, direct observation is required. This is an additional caution.
This nuance may not be universally applied.

As for death, in al-Bazzaziyya: death is like killing, and perhaps killing is like death, as in al-Khulasa and Khizanat al-Muftiyin. Apparently, testimony on killing by hearsay is permissible, but in absolute terms it is problematic because it entails retaliation (qisas) and has doubt; hence it cannot prove something that is averted by doubt. I have not seen anyone clarify this until now. It appears to me that the analogy is only in a specific aspect: the permissibility of a woman observing her iddah upon being informed of his killing like his death, for remarriage, even if the context contradicts it. Similarly, conflicting reports with us about his killing and his life. The author indicated that the woman acts on hearsay primarily, as in al-Bazzaziyya: A man said to a woman, 'I heard that your husband died.' She may marry if the informant is just. And the issues of conflicting reports about his death and his life are here. And his absolute expression about death indicates there is no difference between whether the deceased is well-known or not. But al-Mi'raj restricted it, citing from Fatawa Rashid al-Din, that he must be a scholar or from the workers; but if he is a merchant or similar, it is not permissible except by direct observation. His statement: (As in al-Bazzaziyya, 'a man said to a woman...') In Jami' al-Fusulayn, from the twelfth chapter: If a just person informs her that her husband died or divorced her three times, she may marry. If a corrupt person informs her, she is cautious. In the just person's report of his death, reliance is only on his report if he said 'I saw him dead' or 'I attended his funeral', not if he said 'someone told me'. And it continues. His statement: (Issues of conflicting reports about his death and his life) i.e., in al-Bazzaziyya, where he said: If one person reports the death of an absent husband and two report his life, if the reporter directly observed death or attended the funeral and is just, she may marry. This applies if they did not give dates, or if they gave dates and the date of death is later; if the date of life is later, the witness of life is more likely. And in Wasiyyat Isam: Two witnesses say that her husband so-and-so died or was killed, and another says he is alive; then death is more likely. Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage deals with hearsay testimony on death (موت) and its legal effects, especially for a woman's iddah and remarriage. It begins by comparing death and killing, noting that hearsay on killing is problematic because it could lead to qisas, which is averted by doubt (شبهة). The author (Ibn Abidin or the commentator) clarifies that the analogy between death and killing is only in the specific context of a woman's iddah upon being informed of her husband's killing, not for establishing liability. The text then cites al-Bazzaziyya: a woman can remarry if a just informant tells her her husband died, but only if the informant claims direct observation (عاينته ميتاً أو شهد جنازته), not if he heard it from someone else. For conflicting reports (تعارض الخبرين): if one reports death and two report life, the wife may marry if the reporter of death directly observed it, provided the dates are not given or the death date is later. If the life date is later, the witness of life is preferred. In Wasiyyat Isam, death is preferred over life when there are conflicting witnesses. Also, al-Mi'raj restricts hearsay on death to cases where the deceased is a scholar or worker, not a merchant, otherwise direct observation is needed. This shows the Hanafi school's caution in relying on hearsay for such serious matters.

Key Terms
تسامع (tasamu'): Hearsay; information received by general report rather than direct observation.
تعارض الخبرين (ta'arud al-khabarayn): Conflict between two reports; a legal situation where evidence contradicts each other, requiring principles of preference (ترجيح).
عدالة (adala): Justice; the quality of being upright and trustworthy, required in a witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only if the informant says 'I saw him dead' or 'I attended his funeral'.
C) Only if two just informants confirm it.
D) Only if the husband is a scholar.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a woman to remarry based on a single just witness who directly observed her husband's death, but requires direct observation and resolves conflicting reports by date or preference for death.

Hearsay Testimony on Waqf: Origin vs. Conditions
Commentary on hearsay testimony, focusing on waqf origin vs. conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the origin (asl) and conditions (shurut) of a waqf for hearsay testimony, state the relied-upon Hanafi position, and identify when necessity (darurah) allows an exception.

Core Idea
Hearsay testimony in waqf is accepted for the origin—not conditions—except when necessity overrides.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school about the scope of hearsay testimony (al-shahada bi al-tasammu') in establishing a waqf (endowment). The central issue is whether hearsay can prove not only the basic fact that a property is a waqf (the origin) but also the detailed conditions of its distribution (the shurut). The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq surveys multiple authorities and reconciles conflicting views, revealing the importance of necessity (darurah) as a qualifying factor. By the end, you will be able to articulate the default rule and its exception with clarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Distinction
The passage distinguishes between 'asl al-waqf' (origin of the endowment) and 'shurut al-waqf' (conditions of the endowment). Recognise that the origin is what validity depends on, while conditions are additional stipulations.
e.g., saying 'this is a waqf on the mosque' is origin; 'first repair, then give to the poor' is condition.
2
Trace the Dominant View
The dominant Hanafi position, attributed to al-Sarakhsi and al-Fadli, is that hearsay testimony is accepted only for the origin, not the conditions. Note the analogy to death: hearsay is needed because waqf persists over time, but conditions are detailed and require direct proof.
Al-Fadli rejects hearsay entirely for waqf, but this is a minority view.
3
Examine the Dissenting View
Some later scholars (al-Mujtaba, al-Mi'raj, Fath al-Qadir) accept hearsay for conditions as well, citing necessity when original witnesses are dead and citing judicial practice of consulting old registers.
This view is considered more permissive but conditional on necessity.
4
Understand the Reconciliation
Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq reconciles the views by stating that acceptance on conditions is only valid in necessity (darurah). When witnesses are alive, direct testimony is required. This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
Necessity here means the death of original witnesses or loss of documents.
5
Apply to the Six Matters Context
The passage also lists the six matters where hearsay is accepted: death, marriage, lineage, appointment of judge, appointment of governor, and waqf origin. Note that conditions of waqf are not among these six, reinforcing the default restriction.
Emancipation and dowry are debated; but waqf conditions are not part of the six.

Book of Testimonies. ... for such the produce from. And it was restricted to the origin of the endowment to exclude its conditions, for it is not accepted in them by hearsay. In al-Bazzaziyya: In a correct endowment, it is accepted by hearsay on its origin, not on its conditions, because it [the endowment] remains through the ages, not its conditions. Everything that the validity of the endowment is attached to and depends on is from its origin, and what validity does not depend on is from the conditions. Al-Fadli explicitly stated that testimony by hearsay is not valid in the endowment, and al-Sarakhsi chose its permissibility on its origin, not on its conditions, by saying 'It is an endowment on this mosque' or 'this cemetery'. If they do not mention that, it is not accepted. The meaning of conditions is that they say 'such amount is dispensed to such after stating the destination'. If this is mentioned, it is not accepted. In al-Fusul al-Imadiyya from the tenth: The chosen view is that testimony by hearsay is not accepted on the conditions of the endowment. In al-Khaniyya at the end of the chapter on claims of endowment from the book of endowment, similar is found, and also in al-Is'af. In al-Mujtaba: The chosen view is that it is accepted on the conditions of the endowment. It was relied upon in al-Mi'raj and strengthened in Fath al-Qadir by his saying: And if you know their saying regarding endowments whose verification has been cut off and whose conditions and expenditures are unknown, that it is dealt with as it was in the registers of the judges, you would not hesitate to approve what is in al-Mujtaba because that is the meaning of verification by hearsay. Its response is that it is acted upon thereby only in necessity, and the claimant is general...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the acceptance of hearsay testimony (al-shahada bi al-tasammu') in the context of waqf (endowment). It is an intra-Hanafi dispute focusing on whether hearsay is accepted for the origin (asl) of the waqf or for its conditions (shurut). The passage begins by clarifying that the restriction to 'origin' excludes conditions, citing al-Bazzaziyya that hearsay is accepted only on the origin, not the conditions. The origin is defined as what the validity of the waqf depends on, such as the declaration of waqf on a specific mosque or cemetery. Conditions, such as distribution of surplus produce after stating the destination, are not accepted by hearsay. The commentary then notes differing opinions: al-Fadli rejects hearsay entirely for waqf; al-Sarakhsi accepts it only on the origin; al-Fusul al-Imadiyya and al-Khaniyya reject it on conditions; al-Mujtaba, followed by al-Mi'raj and Fath al-Qadir, accepts it on conditions. The author reconciles these by noting that acceptance on conditions is only in necessity (darurah), when original witnesses are dead. The passage also extends to the six matters for which hearsay is accepted: death, marriage, lineage, appointment of judge, appointment of governor, and waqf (origin). There is discussion whether 'itq (emancipation) and mahr (dowry) are included, with varying transmissions from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The final part deals with the conditions of the informant: no requirement of specific wording (shahida) except when testifying before a judge, and a dispute on whether one trustworthy person suffices (for death) or two are required (for marriage, lineage, etc.). The relied-upon view is that hearsay testimony for waqf conditions is not accepted except in necessity, and that the informant must be trustworthy but not necessarily numerated or male, though for matters other than death, two just witnesses are required.

Key Terms
الشهادة بالتسامع (al-shahada bi al-tasammu'): Testimony based on common knowledge or hearsay, not on direct witnessing. Accepted in specific matters like death and marriage to facilitate proof in recurring events.
أصل الوقف (asl al-waqf): The core act of establishing a waqf, such as the declaration that a property is a religious endowment, without specifying detailed conditions of distribution.
شرائط الوقف (shurut al-waqf): The detailed conditions and allocations of the revenue of a waqf, e.g., 'first so much to repair, then to the poor, etc.' Not essential to validity.
الأشياء الستة (al-ashya' al-sitta): The six matters in which hearsay testimony is accepted: death, marriage, lineage, appointment of a judge, appointment of a governor, and establishment of a waqf (origin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon opinion in the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, is hearsay testimony (al-shahada bi al-tasammu') accepted for the conditions (shurut) of a waqf?
A) Yes, it is absolutely accepted.
B) No, it is never accepted under any circumstances.
C) It is accepted only for the origin of the waqf, not for conditions, except in cases of necessity.
D) It is accepted for conditions if the informant is a judge.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, hearsay testimony is accepted for the origin of a waqf but not for its conditions, except when necessity (death of witnesses) compels an exception.

Foundational Rules of Hearsay Testimony
Establishing the six matters and conditions for hearsay testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six matters where hearsay testimony is accepted in the Hanafi school and identify the required number of trustworthy informants for each matter.

Core Idea
Hearsay testimony is accepted in six specific matters, but death is treated differently: one trustworthy informant suffices.

Hearsay testimony (al-shahada bi al-tasamu') allows a person to testify to a fact they did not directly witness, based on credible reports. The Hanafi school carefully limits this to six areas where direct witness is often impractical. This lesson explores those six matters, the conditions for acceptance, and the key disagreements within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six matters of hearsay
From the text, list the six specific matters where hearsay testimony is accepted: death, marriage, lineage, wala, emancipation, and judicial appointment.
The restriction to these six implies non-acceptance in other matters like mere dower or entry.
2
State the general rule and exceptions
For death, one trustworthy informant suffices. For the other five matters, two trustworthy informants are required. This is the relied-upon position.
Some later Hanafi scholars equated death with the others, but the mufta bihi differentiates.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipation
Imam Abu Yusuf accepts hearsay in emancipation if the slave is well-known; Imam Muhammad does not require this condition. The mufta bihi follows Abu Yusuf.
The dispute extends to wala and dower as well.
4
Determine the role of the word 'shahida'
When informing outside court, the word 'shahida' (I testify) is not required. However, when actually testifying before a judge, it is mandatory.
This applies only to the five matters (except death) that require two informants.
5
Apply the differentiation between death and other matters
Understand the reasoning: death may occur in places where only one person is present; marriage and lineage typically occur among groups, so two informants are expected.
This is the basis for the distinction in the school.

The book of testimonies. The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) and the shaykhs analogized it to death as in al-Khulasa. The restriction to the six mentioned things indicates that it is not accepted in other matters like wala and emancipation. The two stallions differed in reporting the disagreement in emancipation. Imam al-Sarakhsi transmitted that it is not accepted by consensus. His teacher Imam al-Halwani transmitted that it is upon the disagreement transmitted in wala. From Abu Yusuf, permissibility in both. As for one who testifies before the judge, he utters the word testimony. As for the three matters in which the testimony of two trustworthy persons is required, it is appropriate that they testify before him with the word testimony. Our master Zahir al-Din said in al-Aqdiya: This is the choice of al-Sadr al-Imam al-Shahid Burhan al-A'imma. In Mukhtasar al-Quduri: Hearsay testimony is only permissible when he is informed by one he trusts. This indicates that the word testimony is not a condition. In Sharh Ibn al-Shihna: The answer in matters of judgement and marriage is similar to the answer in lineage. They all differentiated between death and the three matters, deeming sufficient the report of one in death but not in them. The difference is that death may occur in a place where there is only one person, unlike the three matters because they typically occur among a group. Some shaykhs did not differentiate. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The correct is that death like marriage and others is not sufficient with the testimony of one. Some shaykhs said: There is no difference between death and the three. The difference in answer is due to difference in the subject matter: the subject matter of the death issue is that he was informed by one trustworthy person, and he did not mention 'trustworthy' in the three. So if the informant in the three is also trustworthy, it becomes permissible for them to testify. Then in the three, when notoriety is established before them by the report of two trustworthy persons, they must inform with the word testimony. In death, since it is established by the report of one by consensus, it is not required; mere information suffices.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes the foundational rules for hearsay testimony (al-shahada bi al-tasamu'). The author begins by noting that hearsay testimony is accepted in six specific matters: death, marriage, lineage, wala, emancipation, and judicial appointment. This restriction implies it is not accepted in other matters like wala and emancipation by analogy. There is a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding emancipation: Imam al-Sarakhsi reports consensus on non-acceptance, while al-Halwani reports it follows the disagreement in wala, with Abu Yusuf allowing it in both. The dower is considered a consequence of marriage, so the stronger opinion allows hearsay testimony for it. Regarding entry (consummation), hearsay is not accepted, but valid seclusion can be used as proof. The author discusses conditions: for acceptance, the informant must be trustworthy. Al-Khassaf, following Abu Yusuf, required that the emancipated slave be well-known in emancipation, but Muhammad did not require this. The word 'shahida' is not required when informing outside court, but it is required when actually testifying before a judge. There is a significant difference between death and the other three matters (marriage, lineage, judicial appointment): for death, one trustworthy informant suffices; for the others, two are needed. Some later scholars equated death with the others, but the relied-upon position is the differentiation.

Key Terms
الشهادة بالتسامع (al-shahada bi al-tasamu'): Testimony based on hearsay, i.e., testifying to a fact that one did not directly witness but learned from others who witnessed it.
العدالة (al-'adala): Trustworthiness, integrity, uprightness of character required in the informant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits hearsay testimony in six specific matters, with death requiring only one trustworthy informant and the other five requiring two.

One Witness to Death and Hearsay Testimony in Hanafi Fiqh
Applying hearsay rules to unusual situations like solitary death witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when a single death witness suffices and apply the hearsay testimony rules to marriage and judgeship scenarios.

Core Idea
One trustworthy witness of death suffices; for other matters, two informants and the word testimony are needed.

This passage addresses a practical procedural dilemma: if only one person witnesses a death, how can proper testimony be given in court? It presents the solution of converting hearsay into direct testimony through a second informant. It then contrasts differing Hanafi views on whether death is treated uniquely and extends the hearsay principle to marriage and judgeship, providing clear examples that prevent false testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem and the Hanafi solution
Read the opening scenario: a single witness of death cannot testify alone. The solution is for that witness to inform another trustworthy person, then both testify together. Note that this turns hearsay into valid testimony.
2
Trace the ikhtilaf on death vs. other matters
Contrast the position in al-Siraj (two required in all except death) with al-Zahiriya (death equal to others). Understand the preferred view (mufta bihi) in Fath al-Qadir: one suffices for death, and 'adala is not required for mutawatir reports.
3
Examine the conditions for hearsay testimony in marriage
Analyze the examples: witnessing the wedding procession (defileh) or two trustworthy informants suffice for testifying to marriage. Also, seeing a couple acting as spouses with public knowledge allows testimony without direct marriage witnessing.
4
Apply the rule to judgeship and warning against fake death rites
Review the analogy: seeing a judge in court suffices for testifying to his judgeship. Contrast with death: performing funeral rites is not enough—direct trustworthy information of actual death is required due to possible mistakes or deceit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الموت مسألة عجيبة هي : إذا لم يعاين الموت إلا واحد ولو شهد عند القاضي لا يقضى بشهادته وحده ماذا يصنع ؟ قالوا : يخبر بذلك عدلاً مثله وإذا سمع منه حل له أن يشهد على موته فيشهد هو مع ذلك الشاهد فيقضى بشهادتهما ا هـ . وظاهر ما في السراج أنه لا بد من خبر عدلين في الكل إلا في الموت، وصحح عن الظهيرية أن الموت كغيره. وفي فتح القدير : المختار الاكتفاء بالواحد في الموت والعدالة إنما تشترط في المخبر في غير المتواتر أما في المتواتر فلا تشترط العدالة ولا لفظ الشهادة كما في الخلاصة. وظاهر كلام المؤلف الاقتصار على الإخبار وهو قصور . قال في الخلاصة : إذا شهد تعريسه وزفافه أو أخبره بذلك عدلان حل له أن يشهد أنها امرأته. وذكر الشارح أنه إذا رأى رجلاً يدخل على امرأته وينبسطان انبساط الأزواج وسمع من الناس أنها زوجته جاز له أن يشهد به وإن لم يعاين النكاح، وكذا إذا رأى شخصاً جالساً مجلس الحكم يفصل الخصومات جاز له أن يشهد على أنه قاض ا هـ . فظاهر الهداية الاكتفاء بما ذكر وذكر غيره أنه لا بد من الإخبار، وفي فتح القدير وهو الحق. وفي المحيط ولو جاء خبر موت إنسان فصنعوا ما يصنع على الميت لم يسعك أن تخبر بموته حتى يخبرك ثقة أنه عاين موته لأن المصائب قد تتقدم على الموت إما خطأ أو غلطاً أو حيلة لقسمة المال ا هـ . وفي القنية : نكاح حضره رجلان ثم أخبر أحدهما جماعة أن فلاناً تزوّج فلانة بإذن وليها ثم الآن يجحد هذا التسامع يجوز للسامعين أن يشهدوا على ذلك اهـ .
And concerning death, there is a strange case: If only one person witnessed the death, and if he were to testify before the judge, the judge would not rule based on his testimony alone. What should he do? They said: He should inform a trustworthy person like himself. When he hears from him, it becomes permissible for him to testify about his death. Then he testifies together with that witness, and the judge rules based on their testimony. The apparent in al-Siraj is that the report of two trustworthy persons is necessary in all matters except death. And it was corrected from al-Zahiriya that death is like others. In Fath al-Qadir: The chosen is to suffice with one in death. 'Adala is only required in the informant in non-mutawatir reports. As for mutawatir, 'adala is not required, nor the word testimony, as in al-Khulasa. And the apparent words of the author restrict to information, which is deficient. He said in al-Khulasa: If one witnesses his being given away in marriage and his wedding procession, or two trustworthy persons inform him of that, he may testify that she is his wife. The commentator mentioned that if he sees a man entering upon his wife, and they are at ease as spouses, and he hears from people that she is his wife, he may testify to that even if he did not witness the marriage. Similarly, if he sees a person sitting in the judgment council deciding disputes, he may testify that he is a judge. So the apparent of al-Hidaya is sufficiency with what was mentioned. Others mentioned that information is necessary, and in Fath al-Qadir that is correct. In al-Muhit: If news comes of the death of a person and they do what is done for the dead, it is not permissible for you to inform of his death until a trustworthy person informs you that he witnessed his death, because calamities may precede death, either by mistake or error or as a trick for division of wealth. In al-Qunniya: A marriage that two men attended, then one of them informed a group that so-and-so married so-and-so with her guardian's permission, and then now he denies this hearsay; it is permissible for the hearers to testify to that.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage addresses practical applications of hearsay testimony, particularly the unusual case where only one person witnessed a death. Normally, court testimony requires two witnesses. The solution is for the solitary witness to inform another trustworthy person; then both can testify together, effectively converting hearsay into direct testimony. This shows the care taken to satisfy procedural requirements. The text then contrasts opinions: al-Siraj requires two in all except death, while al-Zahiriya equates death to other matters. Fath al-Qadir chooses to suffice with one witness for death, noting that 'adala (trustworthiness) is required only in non-mutawatir reports. For mutawatir (widespread) reports, neither 'adala nor the word testimony is needed. The author's statement is criticized as being too restrictive. The passage also gives practical examples: if one sees a wedding procession or hears from two trustworthy people that a marriage occurred, he can testify. Similarly, if one sees a man and woman acting as spouses and hears from people that they are married, he can testify. The same applies to seeing a judge in court. However, relying solely on seeing funeral rites for death is not sufficient; one must have direct information of actual death from a trustworthy witness, as people may mistakenly or deceitfully perform funeral rites before actual death. Finally, a case from al-Qunniya shows that if a person initially informs others of a marriage and later denies it, the hearers can still testify based on that initial information.

Key Terms
التسامع (al-tasamu'): Hearsay, i.e., knowledge acquired through common report or information from others.
المتواتر (al-mutawatir): A report transmitted by such a large number that it is impossible for them to have conspired to fabricate it, rendering the news certain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where only one person witnessed a death, what is the solution to validly testify in court?
A) The single witness testifies alone and the judge accepts it
B) The witness informs another trustworthy person, then both testify together
C) The judge sends someone to investigate
D) The witness must bring a written document

Key Takeaway
A single trustworthy death witness can enable valid testimony by informing a second person, while for other matters two informants using testimony wording are required.

Hearsay Testimony in Inheritance: When the Judgment Stands and When It Falls
Resolving contradictory hearsay judgments in inheritance and lineage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hearsay testimony in inheritance and distinguish when a judgment based on hearsay is binding versus when it can be overturned.

Core Idea
A hearsay-based judgment on lineage is binding on all, but inheritance distribution can be corrected if a closer heir appears.

Hearsay testimony (shahadat al-tasamu') is a form of proof accepted in certain matters where direct evidence is hard to obtain, such as lineage, marriage, freedom, and wala. However, when a closer heir emerges after a judgment based on hearsay, the inheritance distribution may be overturned, but the lineage relationship remains. This lesson examines the nuance from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, focusing on the case of a nephew inheriting through hearsay and the subsequent appearance of a son.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four matters of binding hearsay judgments
The passage states that judgments based on hearsay are binding on the public in four matters: lineage, marriage, freedom, and wala. These rulings cannot be overturned because they establish a status that affects everyone.
2
State the general rule of non-nullification
Generally, once a judgment is made on these matters, it is final and not subject to nullification, as it was made on the public at large.
3
Apply the exception from al-Muhit
An exception arises when a heir closer than the one named appears. For example, if a nephew was declared heir through hearsay, and later a son proves his relationship, the judgment on inheritance is overturned because the son takes precedence. However, the lineage of the nephew as a cousin remains intact.
The son's evidence overrides the inheritance distribution but does not erase the lineage bond.
4
Differentiate between lineage and inheritance in nullification
The passage emphasizes that the nullification is limited to the inheritance portion. The lineage of the first claimant (now a cousin) continues to be valid, so he may inherit from the son if the son dies without closer heirs.
This preserves the possibility of future inheritance from the new heir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن القضاء بالنسب مما لا يقبل النقض لكونه على الكافة كالنكاح والحرية والولاء كما في الصغرى وكذا كتبنا في الفوائد أن القضاء على الكافة في الأربعة لكن يستثنى من النسب ما في المحيط من باب الشهادة بالتسامع : شهدا أن فلان بن فلان مات وهذا ابن أخيه ووارثه قضى بالنسب والإرث، ثم أقام آخر البينة أنه ابن الميت ووارثه ينقض الأوّل ويقضى للثاني لأن الابن مقدم على ابن الأخ، ولا تنافي بين الأوّل والثاني لجواز أن يكون له ابن وابن أخ فينقض القضاء في حق الميراث لا في حق النسب حتى يبقى الأول ابن عم له حتى يرث منه إذا مات ولم يترك وارثاً آخر أقرب منه، فإنه أقام آخر البينة أن الميت الأوّل فلان بن فلان ونسبه إلى أب أخر غير الأب الذي نسبه إلى الأوّل فإنه ينظر؛ إن ادعى ابن أخيه لا ينقض القضاء الأوّل لأنه لما أثبت نفسه من الأوّل خرج عن هذه أن الثلاثة، فلو كان المخبر في الثلاثة عدلاً أيضاً حل لهم أن يشهدوا ثم في الثلاثة إذا ثبت الشهرة عندهما بخبر عدلين يجب الإخبار بلفظ الشهادة وفي الموت لما ثبت بخبر الواحد بالإجماع لا يجب بل يكتفى بمجرد الإخبار .
Then know that judgment concerning lineage is not subject to nullification because it is binding on the public like marriage, freedom, and wala, as in al-Sughra. Similarly we wrote in al-Fawa'id that judgment binding on the public is in four matters, but an exception from lineage is what is in al-Muhit from the chapter of hearsay testimony: Two witnesses testified that so-and-so son of so-and-so died and this is his nephew and heir, and the judge ruled based on lineage and inheritance. Then another person brought evidence that he is the son of the deceased and his heir. The first judgment is nullified and judgment is given for the second, because the son takes precedence over the nephew. There is no contradiction between the first and second, because it is possible that he had both a son and a nephew. So the judgment is nullified concerning inheritance, not concerning lineage. Thus the first remains as a cousin to him, so that he inherits from him if he dies without leaving any other closer heir. If another brings evidence that the first deceased is so-and-so son of so-and-so and traces his lineage to a different father than the one to which the first traced his lineage, then he investigates. If he claims that his nephew is not nullified, the first judgment is not nullified because when he established himself from the first, he left from these three. If the informant in the three is also trustworthy, it becomes permissible for them to testify. Then in the three, when notoriety is established before them by the report of two trustworthy persons, they must inform with the word testimony. In death, since it is established by the report of one by consensus, it is not required; mere information suffices.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage discusses the finality of judgments based on hearsay testimony, particularly regarding lineage and inheritance. Generally, judgments about lineage, marriage, freedom, and wala are binding on the public and cannot be overturned. However, there is an exception from al-Muhit: if a judgment was made that a person is the nephew and heir of the deceased based on hearsay, and then someone else appears with evidence that he is actually the son, the inheritance part of the judgment is overturned because the son is a closer heir. But the lineage portion remains—the nephew is now considered a cousin of the son, and may inherit from the son if the son dies without closer heirs. If a third person claims a different lineage entirely, the judge must investigate. The text ends by reiterating the earlier rule about needing two informants for the three matters (marriage, lineage, etc.) and one for death. This shows the nuanced handling of hearsay testimony in inheritance, balancing the need for finality in lineage with the correct distribution of inheritance.

Key Terms
القضاء على الكافة (al-qada' 'ala al-kaffa): A judgment that is binding on all persons and cannot be overturned because it establishes a status (lineage, marriage, freedom, wala).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a judgment based on hearsay declares a person as the nephew and heir of the deceased, and later a son appears with evidence, what happens?
A) The entire judgment is overturned, including lineage.
B) The inheritance judgment is overturned but the lineage remains.
C) The son is ignored because the judgment is final.
D) The case is re-evaluated by a different judge.

Key Takeaway
A hearsay-based judgment on lineage is permanent, but its inheritance consequences can be corrected if a closer heir proves their right.

Testimony by Hearsay in Death, Marriage, and Judgeship
Explains ruling on testimony by hearsay for death, marriage, judgeship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony by hearsay for death, marriage, and judgeship, and identify the number of informants required for each according to the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Hearsay testimony is accepted for death with one informant; for marriage and judgeship with two.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical problem: how to prove death when only one person witnessed it. It also extends the same reasoning to marriage and judgeship. The Hanafi school has an internal difference on the number of informants needed, and we will see the preferred view settled by later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem and its solution for death
Read the opening: if only one person witnesses a death, that person cannot testify alone. The solution: he informs another trustworthy person, then they testify together, making two witnesses.
This is the basic mechanism of hearsay testimony for death.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the number of informants
Al-Siraj requires two informants for all matters except death. Al-Dhahiriyya holds death like other matters, requiring two. The chosen view (mufta bihi) from Fath al-Qadir is that one informant suffices for death.
The dispute centers on whether death is a unique case requiring special leniency.
3
Extend to marriage
For marriage, testimony can be based on direct witnessing of the wedding (tashammu' of the event) or on information from two trustworthy persons. Seeing a couple living as spouses with common knowledge also suffices.
The text cites al-Khulasa and the commentator's view on observing marital intimacy.
4
Apply to judgeship
If a person sees someone sitting in a court and deciding disputes, he may testify that the person is a judge. This does not require direct knowledge of the appointment.
This is analogous to marriage: common observation suffices.
5
Recognize the caution in al-Muhit and the rule in al-Qunniyya
Mere news of death without direct eyewitness confirmation is insufficient; one must be informed by a trustworthy person who witnessed the death. However, if two witnesses attend a marriage and one informs others, those hearers may testify even if the informant later denies it.
These additional passages highlight the need for reliable transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الموت مسألة عجيبة هي : إذا لم يعاين الموت إلا واحد ولو شهد عند القاضي لا يقضى بشهادته وحده ماذا يصنع ؟ قالوا : يخبر بذلك عدلاً مثله وإذا سمع منه حل له أن يشهد على موته فيشهد هو مع ذلك الشاهد فيقضى بشهادتهما ا هـ . وظاهر ما في السراج أنه لا بد من خبر عدلين في الكل إلا في الموت، وصحح عن الظهيرية أن الموت كغيره. وفي فتح القدير : المختار الاكتفاء بالواحد في الموت والعدالة إنما تشترط في المخبر في غير المتواتر أما في المتواتر فلا تشترط العدالة ولا لفظ الشهادة كما في الخلاصة. وظاهر كلام المؤلف الاقتصار على الإخبار وهو قصور . قال في الخلاصة : إذا شهد تعريسه وزفافه أو أخبره بذلك عدلان حل له أن يشهد أنها امرأته. وذكر الشارح أنه إذا رأى رجلاً يدخل على امرأته وينبسطان انبساط الأزواج وسمع من الناس أنها زوجته جاز له أن يشهد به وإن لم يعاين النكاح، وكذا إذا رأى شخصاً جالساً مجلس الحكم يفصل الخصومات جاز له أن يشهد على أنه قاض ا هـ . فظاهر الهداية الاكتفاء بما ذكر وذكر غيره أنه لا بد من الإخبار، وفي فتح القدير وهو الحق. وفي المحيط ولو جاء خبر موت إنسان فصنعوا ما يصنع على الميت لم يسعك أن تخبر بموته حتى يخبرك ثقة أنه عاين موته لأن المصائب قد تتقدم على الموت إما خطأ أو غلطاً أو حيلة لقسمة المال ا هـ . وفي القنية : نكاح حضره رجلان ثم أخبر أحدهما جماعة أن فلاناً تزوّج فلانة بإذن وليها ثم الآن يجحد هذا التسامع يجوز للسامعين أن يشهدوا على ذلك اهـ .
And regarding death, there is a remarkable issue: if only one person witnessed the death, and even if he testifies before the judge, the judge does not rule based on his testimony alone; what should be done? They said: he should inform a trustworthy person like himself, and when he hears from him, it becomes permissible for him to testify about his death, so he testifies along with that witness, and the judge rules based on their testimony. The apparent meaning of what is in al-Siraj is that news from two trustworthy persons is necessary in everything except death, and it is authenticated from al-Dhahiriyya that death is like others. In Fath al-Qadir: the chosen view is sufficiency of one in death; and integrity is only required in the informant in non-mutawatir matters; as for mutawatir, integrity is not required, nor the wording of testimony, as in al-Khulasa. The apparent meaning of the author's statement is limiting to informing, and that is insufficient. He said in al-Khulasa: if one witnesses his wedding and bridal procession, or two trustworthy persons inform him of that, it becomes permissible for him to testify that she is his wife. And the commentator mentioned that if he sees a man entering upon his wife and they are intimate with the intimacy of spouses, and he hears from the people that she is his wife, it is permissible for him to testify to that even if he did not witness the marriage contract. Similarly, if he sees a person sitting in the court and deciding disputes, it is permissible for him to testify that he is a judge. The apparent meaning of al-Hidaya is sufficiency with what was mentioned, and others mentioned that informing is necessary, and in Fath al-Qadir that is the truth. In al-Muhit: if news of a person's death comes and they do what is done for the deceased, it is not permissible for you to inform of his death until a trustworthy person informs you that he witnessed his death, because calamities may precede death, either mistakenly, erroneously, or as a trick for division of wealth. And in al-Qunniyya: a marriage attended by two men, then one of them informed a group that so-and-so married so-and-so with the permission of her guardian, and now he denies this hearsay; it is permissible for the hearers to testify about that.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on shahada bil tashammu' (testimony by hearsay) in three specific cases: death, marriage, and judgeship. The key issue is how to testify when one has not directly witnessed the event. For death, if only one person witnessed it, that person cannot testify alone but can inform another trustworthy person, who then testifies alongside the first. The text notes a disagreement: al-Siraj requires two informants for all except death, while al-Dhahiriyya makes death like others. The chosen view in Fath al-Qadir is that one informant suffices for death, and that integrity is not required for mutawatir matters. For marriage, testimony can be based either on direct witnessing of the wedding or on the information of two trustworthy persons. Similarly, for judgeship, seeing a person act as a judge suffices. The text emphasizes that mere news of death without direct confirmation is insufficient; one must be informed by a trustworthy eyewitness. The excerpt from al-Qunniyya shows that if two witnesses attend a marriage and one later informs others, those hearers may testify if the informant later denies it.

Key Terms
التسامع (al-tashammu'): Hearsay testimony based on common knowledge or information from others, not direct observation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in Fath al-Qadir, how many trustworthy informants are required to testify to a person's death?
A) Two informants are required.
B) One informant suffices.
C) No informant is needed; anyone can testify.
D) Only direct witness can testify.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts hearsay testimony for death with one informant, and for marriage and judgeship with two informants or widespread knowledge.
